Divine Decree

Divine Decree: Good and Evil Are from Allah
Defines qadr and the obligation to be content with it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of qada and qadr, why contentment with it is obligatory, and the implications of both good and evil being from Allah.

Core Idea
Both good and evil in divine decree are from Allah, requiring contentment and acceptance.

This lesson introduces the core belief about divine decree (qadr). The author affirms that both good and evil in qadr are from Allah, and explains what qada and qadr encompass. This teaching directly counters the idea that evil cannot be attributed to Allah, and establishes the believer's attitude of contentment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the key terms: القضاء, القدر, الرضاء.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Define qada and qadr
The passage defines qada and qadr as the assignment of every creature to its station, covering moral and physical aspects, including reward and punishment.
Notice that it includes both good and evil, benefit and harm.
3
Understand the obligation of contentment
Because the decree is unalterable and from Allah, contentment is obligatory. Reflect on why this is important for faith.
4
Address the misconception
The passage explicitly states that both good and evil are from Allah, correcting the mistaken belief that evil is not from Him.
See the common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(والقدر) أي وبالقضاء والقدر خيره) وشرّه أي نفعه وضرّه وحلوه ومره حال كونه (من تعالى فلا تغيير للتقدير، فيجب الرضاء بالقضاء والقدر، وهو تعيين كل مخلوق بمرتبته التي توجد من حسن وقبح ونفع وضر، وما يحيط به من مكان وزمان وما يترتب عليه من ثواب أو عقاب.
(Al-Qadr) that is, with al-Qada' and al-Qadr (qada and qadr), its good and its evil, that is, its benefit and harm, its sweet and bitter, in the state of its being (from the Exalted, so there is no alteration of the decree; thus contentment with qada and qadr is obligatory, and it is the assignment of every creature to its station in which it exists, of good and evil, benefit and harm, and what surrounds it of place and time, and what results from it of reward or punishment.)
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage provides a definition of divine decree (al-qadr) and its ruling. The text glosses 'al-qadr' as including both qada and qadr, and its good and evil are further explained as benefit and harm, sweet and bitter. The source of both good and evil is from Allah the Exalted, with the consequence that no change can occur in the decree. Therefore, the believer must be content with qada and qadr. The passage then defines qada and qadr as the assignment of every creature to its specific station, encompassing both moral qualities (good and evil) and physical aspects (benefit, harm, place, time), and also the eternal consequences of reward or punishment that result from that station.

Key Terms
القضاء
القدر
الرضاء
التقدير

عقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Evil cannot be from Allah.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that both good and evil in qadr are from Allah, so one must accept both.

Checkpoint
What is the implication of affirming that good and evil in qadr are from Allah?
A) One may object to evil decrees.
B) No change is possible in the decree, and contentment is obligatory.
C) Evil is not truly from Allah.
D) Only good is from Allah; evil is from human actions.

Key Takeaway
Both good and evil in divine decree are from Allah, and contentment with qada and qadr is an obligation for every believer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever blamed Allah for something bad that happened to you, thinking it should not have been? How does knowing that both good and evil are from Allah challenge your complaint?
?
Are you truly content with Allah's decree, even the parts that cause you pain? What specific area of your life do you struggle to accept as from Allah, and what step will you take today to cultivate that contentment?

Objection on Divine Will and Human Disobedience
Raises objection that God's command to Pharaoh should have been fulfilled if irada and mashi'a are identical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection that arises if God's will (irada) and decree (mashi'a) are identical, and the initial response that distinguishes between types of divine requests.

Core Idea
The command to believe is a request based on choice, not an irresistible decree.

This lesson presents a key objection against the identity of irada and mashi'a. If God's will is the same as His decree, then His command to Pharaoh and Abu Jahl to believe should have been fulfilled. However, they did not believe. The lesson then begins to unpack the answer, which distinguishes between a request based on choice and a decree that compels.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the objector states a premise (if irada and mashi'a are one) and a consequence (then belief would have been found), and contrasts it with reality (they did not believe).
The passage is incomplete; the answer cuts off mid-sentence.
2
Understand the logical problem
The objection claims that if irada and mashi'a are identical, then God's command to believe should have been an act of creation (mashi'a = bringing into existence). Since they did not believe, the identity is false.
This is a classic theological dilemma about divine will and human free will.
3
Notice the beginning of the answer
The answer starts by stating that God's request is of two types. The text begins to describe the first type: 'a request from the obligated one on the basis of choice' (طلب من المكلف على وجه الاختيار). This suggests that some divine requests are not decrees but offers that allow human choice.
The full answer is not provided, but the distinction is the key to resolving the objection.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why distinguishing between types of requests resolves the objection. If the command to believe is a request on the basis of choice, then it does not compel belief, so human disobedience does not contradict God's will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: إن الله تعالى طلب الإيمان من فرعون وأبي جهل وأمثالهما بالأمر ولم يوجد منهم الإيمان، فلو كانت الإرادة والمشيئة واحدة كما زعمتم لوجد ذلك منهم؛ لأن المشيئة هي الإيجاد. الله تعالى على نوعين : طلب من المكلف على وجه الاختيار وهو المسمى قلنا: الطلب من
For if it is said: Indeed, God, exalted, sought faith from Pharaoh, Abu Jahl, and their likes through the command, and faith was not found from them. So if will (irada) and decree (mashi'a) were one as you claim, it would have been found from them, because mashi'a is bringing into existence. [But we say:] The request from God, exalted, is of two types: a request from the obligated one on the basis of choice, which is called... We said: the request from
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the claim that irada and mashi'a are identical in God. The objector points out that God commanded Pharaoh and Abu Jahl to believe, but they did not believe. If irada and mashi'a were one, then His command should have been an act of bringing into existence, so they would have believed. The answer begins: 'We said: the request from God, exalted, is of two types: a request from the obligated one on the basis of choice, which is called...' but unfortunately the text cuts off. The incomplete answer suggests a distinction between a coercive request and a request that allows choice. The author seems to be saying that the command to believe is a request on the basis of choice, not an irresistible decree. The full argument would be found in the original source.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection confuses divine command with divine will; the answer distinguishes between types of divine request.
Correction: Not all divine requests are decrees. Some are calls that allow human free will, so the lack of belief does not disprove the identity of irada and mashi'a.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the objection regarding Pharaoh and Abu Jahl?
A) They were not commanded to believe
B) They believed, so the objection is false
C) God commanded them but they did not believe, which seems to contradict the unity of irada and mashi'a
D) They were forced to disbelieve

Key Takeaway
The objection challenges the identity of irada and mashi'a, but the answer begins to resolve it by distinguishing between a divine request based on choice and a decree that compels.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you insist on a sin despite knowing Allah's command, do you ever silently accuse Him of not truly willing your obedience? How does this lesson dismantle that excuse?
?
If Allah's command to you is a request based on choice, what does your persistent disobedience say about the condition of your will? Will you today respond to His command as an act of love, not compulsion?
?
You believe in Qadar. But does your heart ever resent Allah for the disbelief of others, as if His decree failed? How does the distinction between command and decree correct your view of Him?

Two Scholarly Positions on Attributing Evil to God's Will
Records a disagreement among Maturidi scholars on phrasing God's will for evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Maturidi positions on whether to say in detail that God wills evil acts, and how both affirm God's absolute will while addressing the problem of evil.

Core Idea
Evil is willed by God, but how we say it matters: globally or with qualifiers.

This passage records an internal Maturidi discussion on a sensitive theological point: how to express that God wills evil acts without implying that He does evil. The scholars differed on whether to specify individual evils or speak only in general terms. As you read, notice how both positions maintain God's universal will while carefully preserving human responsibility and God's perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to grasp the two opinions.
Notice the key terms: إجمال (summary) and تفصيل (detail).
2
Identify the first opinion's analogy
The first group uses the example of God creating carcasses and filth. Understand how this analogy supports avoiding detailed attribution.
They analogize from creation to will: just as we don't say 'God created filth' in detail, we shouldn't say 'God willed evil' in detail.
3
Understand the second opinion's qualifying phrase
The second group permits detail by adding the qualifier 'kasban lahu' (as an acquisition for him). See how this preserves human responsibility.
The qualifier distinguishes God's will from His approval—He wills the act as an acquisition, but the act remains evil and forbidden.
4
Connect to the Maturidi-Ash'ari consensus
Note that the passage ends by stating that the second opinion is the choice of al-Maturidi and also held by al-Ash'ari, showing broad agreement.
Despite the disagreement, both schools ultimately affirm God's universal will over all actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلفت عبارات أصحابنا رحمهم الله في هذه المسألة قال بعضهم: نقول: إن جميع الموجودات والأفعال مراد الله تعالى ولا نقول على التفصيل: إن القبائح والشرور والمعاصي من الله، كما نقول على الإجمال: إنه خالق لجميع الموجودات، ولا نقول على التفصيل: إنه خالق الجيف والقاذورات. وقال بعضهم: نقول على التفصيل ولكن مقروناً بقرينة تليق به، فنقول: إنه أراد الكفر من الكافر كسبا له شراً قبيحاً منهيًا عنه، كما أراد الإيمان من المؤمن كسبا له خيرا حسنا مأمورا به فهو اختيار الماتريدي، وبه قال الأشعري.
Then the expressions of our scholars (may God have mercy on them) differed in this matter. Some of them said: We say: All existents and actions are willed by God, but we do not say in detail that ugly acts, evils, and sins are from God, just as we say in summary that He is the Creator of all existents, and we do not say in detail that He is the Creator of carcasses and filth. And some of them said: We say in detail, but coupled with a qualifying phrase befitting Him. So we say: He willed disbelief from the disbeliever as an acquisition (kasb) for him, being evil, ugly, and forbidden, just as He willed faith from the believer as an acquisition for him, being good, beautiful, and commanded. That is the choice of al-Maturidi, and al-Ash'ari said the same.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage records a disagreement among Hanafi-Maturidi scholars about how to phrase the attribution of evil acts to God's will. The first group takes a cautious approach: they affirm globally that all things are willed by God, but they avoid specifying in detail that particular evils (ugly acts, sins, carcasses, filth) are directly from God. They draw an analogy with creation: God is the Creator of all existents in general, but they avoid saying 'God created carcasses' in detail because of the inappropriate association. The second group (which the author identifies as the Maturidi choice, also held by al-Ash'ari) permits detailed attribution as long as it is accompanied by appropriate qualifiers. For example, one may say that God willed the disbeliever's disbelief 'as an acquisition for him' (kasban lahu), and describe that disbelief as evil, ugly, and forbidden. Similarly, faith is willed as a good acquisition. This position carefully maintains God's absolute will while preserving the moral quality of actions as acquired by humans. The passage thus shows a nuanced commitment to both divine decree and human responsibility.

Key Terms
كسب
قبائح
شرور
معاصي
إجمال
تفصيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Maturidis avoid saying God wills evil at all.
Correction: The second position shows they do say God wills evil in detail, but with qualifiers like 'as an acquisition' and describing the act as evil and forbidden, so that God's will is affirmed without attributing evil directly to Him.

Checkpoint
What is the Maturidi position on saying that God willed specific evil acts?
A) They deny that God wills evil in any sense
B) They only affirm God's will globally, never in detail
C) They affirm it in detail but with qualifiers like 'as an acquisition' and describing the act as evil
D) They hold that God does not will acts at all

Key Takeaway
Maturidi scholars agree that God wills all things, including evil, but disagree on how to articulate this: some avoid detailed mention, others add qualifiers like 'as an acquisition' to preserve moral responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face something evil in your life, do you hesitate to attribute it to God's will because it feels uncomfortable? How does this lesson challenge your hesitation?
?
Are you more like the first group (avoiding detailed mention) or the second (adding qualifiers)? Which approach brings you closer to God's reality while preserving His transcendence?
?
How can you train your tongue to speak about God's will and evil with the precision taught here, especially when calamities strike? What would you actually say in a moment of trial?

Creation from Nothing (Ex Nihilo)
The passage declares and defends the creation of all things from nothing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic doctrine of creation ex nihilo and how it distinguishes Allah's transcendence from any pre-existing matter.

Core Idea
Allah created the universe from nothing, without any pre-existing material or model.

This lesson focuses on one of the foundational beliefs in Islam: that Allah created all things from absolute nothingness. The passage from the commentary explains what 'from nothing' means and addresses potential misunderstandings about secondary causation. It affirms that Allah alone is the Creator of every atom and every state, and that He existed before all things without any partner.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the author defines 'creation from nothing' and supports it with Quranic verses.
2
Identify the key proof texts
The passage cites two verses: 'Faatir al-samaawaati wa al-ard' (6:14) and 'Allah is the Creator of everything' (39:62). Reflect on how each supports the doctrine.
These verses are central to the argument.
3
Reflect on the implication that Allah is the only creator
The passage emphasizes that even when things are created from other materials, the original matter was created from nothing. Consider why this preserves Allah's sole sovereignty.
This counters the misconception that secondary causes diminish Divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول في القدر: خلق الله تعالى الأشياء لا من شيء، وكان الله تعالى عالماً في الأزل بالأشياء قبل كونها، وهو الذي قدر الأشياء وقضاها. (خلق الله تعالى الأشياء من الذوات والحالات كالسكون والحركات والأنوار والظلمات والشرور والخيرات والعلويات والسفليات لا من شيء) أي لا من مادة سابقة على المخلوقات لقوله تعالى: ﴿ فَاطِرِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ ﴾ [الأنعام: ١٤]، أي مبدعهما ومخترعهما من غير مثال سبق له فيهما حال ابتدائهما وإنشائهما، ولا ينافيه أنه خلق بعض الأشياء من بعض المواد على وفق ما أراد، فإن أصول تلك المواد خلقت من غير وجود شيء في عالم الكون والفساد. ولو تصور وجود الشيء فهو تحت خلق الخالق لقوله تعالى : ﴿ اللهُ خَالِقُ كُلّ شَيْء ﴾ [الزمر: ٦٢]، ولأنه سبحانه كان ولم يكن معه شيء، بل في نظر العارفين هو الآن على ما كان فهو منزّه عن أن يكون له شريك في الخلق والفعل والمادة، ولو في إيجاد ذرّة أو إمدادها بسكون أو حركة. (وكان الله تعالى عالماً في الأزل بالأشياء قبل كونها) أي قبل وجود الأشياء وتحققها في عالم الإبداع، وهذا معنى قوله تعالى: ﴿وَكَانَ اللهُ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ عَلِيمًا ﴾ [الفتح: ٢٦]، وما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه، فلا يحتاج إلى أن يقال: "كان" زائدة أو رابطة، وهو الذي قدر الأشياء وقضاها.
The discourse on al-qadr (divine decree): Allah Most High created things not from something, and Allah Most High was eternally knowing of things before their coming into existence, and He is the one who measured and decreed things. (He created things from substances and states such as motion and rest, lights and darknesses, evils and goods, celestial and terrestrial, not from something) i.e., not from matter preceding the created things, as per His saying: 'Originator of the heavens and the earth' [al-An'am 6:14], i.e., their Originator and Inventor without any prior example for them at the time of their beginning and creation. And it does not contradict that He created some things from some materials according to what He willed, for the origins of those materials were created without the existence of anything in the world of generation and corruption. And if the existence of a thing is conceived, it is under the creation of the Creator, as per His saying: 'Allah is the Creator of everything' [al-Zumar 39:62], and because He, glorified, existed and nothing was with Him; rather, in the view of the knowers, He is now as He was. So He is transcendent above having a partner in creation, action, and matter, even in bringing into existence an atom or supplying it with rest or motion. (And Allah Most High was eternally knowing of things before their coming into existence) i.e., before the existence of things and their realization in the world of invention, and this is the meaning of His saying: 'And Allah is of all things Knowing' [al-Fath 48:26]. And what has been established as eternal, its non-existence is impossible, so there is no need to say that 'kaana' is redundant or connective. And He is the one who measured and decreed things.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a theological statement and commentary on the topic of qadr (divine decree). It begins by declaring the Sunni doctrine: Allah created things from nothing, He was eternally knowing of things before they existed, and He is the one who measured and decreed them. The parenthetical commentary then expands: 'He created things from substances and states... not from something' means that the original creation was not from any pre-existing matter, as indicated by the verse 'Originator of the heavens and the earth' (6:14), which implies bringing them into existence without a prior model. This does not contradict the fact that He later created some things from other materials (like Adam from clay), because the origins of those materials themselves were created from nothing. The commentary emphasizes that any existence whatsoever is under Allah's creation, citing 'Allah is the Creator of everything' (39:62), and the principle that before creation, Allah existed alone with nothing else. Moreover, in the view of the true knowers, Allah is now as He was before creation—unchanged and transcendent. Thus He has no partner in creation, action, or matter, not even in creating an atom or its properties. The passage then continues the commentary: 'And Allah was eternally knowing...' means that His knowledge of things preceded their existence in the world of ibda' (invention). This is the meaning of the verse 'And Allah is of all things Knowing' (48:26). Because His knowledge is eternal, it cannot cease; thus the word 'kaana' in such verses does not need to be considered pleonastic or a copula—it indicates that His knowledge has always existed. The conclusion is that Allah is the one who measured and decreed all things.

Key Terms
القدر
خلق
لا من شيء
المادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The doctrine of creation from nothing denies that Allah creates through secondary causes (e.g., creating humans from clay).
Correction: The passage explicitly states that creating some things from materials does not contradict creation ex nihilo, because the materials themselves were originally created from nothing.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which verse is cited to support that Allah created the heavens and the earth without a prior example?
A) 'Allah is the Creator of everything' (39:62)
B) 'Originator of the heavens and the earth' (6:14)
C) 'And Allah is of all things Knowing' (48:26)
D) 'He measured and decreed things'

Key Takeaway
Allah alone is the Creator of everything, and He originated all matter from nothing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah alone creates every atom and every movement you make, what does that mean for the pride you feel in your achievements? Where have you been attributing success to your own skill while forgetting that Allah is the direct Creator of that action? How can you today consciously attribute every good action to His creation?
?
The passage states that Allah is now as He was—continuously creating without any change. Do you live your life as if God has set the world in motion and left it, or as if He is actively creating your next breath? What would change in your daily awareness if you truly believed that Allah is creating your rest, your speech, and even your thoughts? How can you remind yourself of this truth every hour?
?
If you struggle with a sinful habit or a state of laziness, and you know that every state (including laziness) is created directly by Allah, does that remove your responsibility? No—you are commanded to acquire actions (kasb). In light of this lesson, what is your first step when you feel a sinful impulse? Will you beg Allah to create in you the action of repentance, or will you give in to the created state? What will you commit to doing the next time that impulse comes?

Eternal Knowledge and Divine Decree
The passage explains Allah's eternal knowledge and its relation to decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's knowledge is eternal and encompasses all things before their existence, and that He measured and decreed all things.

Core Idea
Allah's eternal knowledge and preordination encompass all creation.

This lesson continues the discourse on qadr, focusing on the attributes of divine knowledge and decree. The passage affirms that Allah knew all things before they existed, and that this knowledge is eternal and unchanging. It also explains that Allah's decree (qadr and qada) is based on His eternal knowledge. The commentary addresses a linguistic nuance about the word 'kaana' and reassures that eternal attributes are essential.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note how the author connects eternal knowledge with the act of decree.
2
Understand the meaning of eternal knowledge
The passage says Allah was knowing of things before their existence. Reflect on why this implies His knowledge is not acquired but inherent and timeless.
3
Consider the decree as an expression of knowledge
The passage ends by stating 'He is the one who measured and decreed things.' Think about how decree follows from eternal knowledge without changing it.
This links to the overall concept of qadr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول في القدر: خلق الله تعالى الأشياء لا من شيء، وكان الله تعالى عالماً في الأزل بالأشياء قبل كونها، وهو الذي قدر الأشياء وقضاها. (خلق الله تعالى الأشياء من الذوات والحالات كالسكون والحركات والأنوار والظلمات والشرور والخيرات والعلويات والسفليات لا من شيء) أي لا من مادة سابقة على المخلوقات لقوله تعالى: ﴿ فَاطِرِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ ﴾ [الأنعام: ١٤]، أي مبدعهما ومخترعهما من غير مثال سبق له فيهما حال ابتدائهما وإنشائهما، ولا ينافيه أنه خلق بعض الأشياء من بعض المواد على وفق ما أراد، فإن أصول تلك المواد خلقت من غير وجود شيء في عالم الكون والفساد. ولو تصور وجود الشيء فهو تحت خلق الخالق لقوله تعالى : ﴿ اللهُ خَالِقُ كُلّ شَيْء ﴾ [الزمر: ٦٢]، ولأنه سبحانه كان ولم يكن معه شيء، بل في نظر العارفين هو الآن على ما كان فهو منزّه عن أن يكون له شريك في الخلق والفعل والمادة، ولو في إيجاد ذرّة أو إمدادها بسكون أو حركة. (وكان الله تعالى عالماً في الأزل بالأشياء قبل كونها) أي قبل وجود الأشياء وتحققها في عالم الإبداع، وهذا معنى قوله تعالى: ﴿وَكَانَ اللهُ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ عَلِيمًا ﴾ [الفتح: ٢٦]، وما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه، فلا يحتاج إلى أن يقال: "كان" زائدة أو رابطة، وهو الذي قدر الأشياء وقضاها.
The discourse on al-qadr (divine decree): Allah Most High created things not from something, and Allah Most High was eternally knowing of things before their coming into existence, and He is the one who measured and decreed things. (He created things from substances and states such as motion and rest, lights and darknesses, evils and goods, celestial and terrestrial, not from something) i.e., not from matter preceding the created things, as per His saying: 'Originator of the heavens and the earth' [al-An'am 6:14], i.e., their Originator and Inventor without any prior example for them at the time of their beginning and creation. And it does not contradict that He created some things from some materials according to what He willed, for the origins of those materials were created without the existence of anything in the world of generation and corruption. And if the existence of a thing is conceived, it is under the creation of the Creator, as per His saying: 'Allah is the Creator of everything' [al-Zumar 39:62], and because He, glorified, existed and nothing was with Him; rather, in the view of the knowers, He is now as He was. So He is transcendent above having a partner in creation, action, and matter, even in bringing into existence an atom or supplying it with rest or motion. (And Allah Most High was eternally knowing of things before their coming into existence) i.e., before the existence of things and their realization in the world of invention, and this is the meaning of His saying: 'And Allah is of all things Knowing' [al-Fath 48:26]. And what has been established as eternal, its non-existence is impossible, so there is no need to say that 'kaana' is redundant or connective. And He is the one who measured and decreed things.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a theological statement and commentary on the topic of qadr (divine decree). It begins by declaring the Sunni doctrine: Allah created things from nothing, He was eternally knowing of things before they existed, and He is the one who measured and decreed them. The parenthetical commentary then expands: 'He created things from substances and states... not from something' means that the original creation was not from any pre-existing matter, as indicated by the verse 'Originator of the heavens and the earth' (6:14), which implies bringing them into existence without a prior model. This does not contradict the fact that He later created some things from other materials (like Adam from clay), because the origins of those materials themselves were created from nothing. The commentary emphasizes that any existence whatsoever is under Allah's creation, citing 'Allah is the Creator of everything' (39:62), and the principle that before creation, Allah existed alone with nothing else. Moreover, in the view of the true knowers, Allah is now as He was before creation—unchanged and transcendent. Thus He has no partner in creation, action, or matter, not even in creating an atom or its properties. The passage then continues the commentary: 'And Allah was eternally knowing...' means that His knowledge of things preceded their existence in the world of ibda' (invention). This is the meaning of the verse 'And Allah is of all things Knowing' (48:26). Because His knowledge is eternal, it cannot cease; thus the word 'kaana' in such verses does not need to be considered pleonastic or a copula—it indicates that His knowledge has always existed. The conclusion is that Allah is the one who measured and decreed all things.

Key Terms
الأزل
علم الله
القضاء
الإبداع

A) Because the word 'kaana' always indicates past tense.

Key Takeaway
Allah's knowledge is eternal and unchanging, and He measures and decrees all things based on that knowledge.

?

The Definition of Qadar and the Distinction between Qada and Qadar
Defines qadar and distinguishes qada from qadar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of qadar as the eternal decree and the distinction between qada (general decree) and qadar (detailed decree).

Core Idea
Qadar is God's eternal decree; qada is the general judgment, qadar the detailed.

In this brief passage, the author summarizes the concept of qadar and clarifies the relationship between two key terms: qada and qadar. Understanding this distinction is essential for navigating discussions on divine decree and human action. Let's see what the text says.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of qadar
Start by reading the first sentence carefully. It defines qadar as what occurs from the servant that was decreed in eternity, encompassing good and evil, sweet and bitter, and attributes it to God's creation and will.
Notice the phrase 'ما شاء كان وما لا فلا' emphasizing God's absolute sovereignty.
2
Understand the distinction between qada and qadar
The second sentence distinguishes qada and qadar: one refers to the general judgment (al-ḥukm al-ijmālī), the other to the detailed judgment (al-ḥukm al-tafṣīlī). Reflect on how these two relate to each other.
This distinction helps clarify that qada is the overarching decree, while qadar specifies the details.
3
Apply the distinction
Think of an example: God's general decree that a person will die is qada; the specific time, place, and manner of death is qadar. This illustrates the relationship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومجمل الأمر أن القدر وهو ما يقع من العبد المقدر في الأزل من خيره وشره وحلوه ومره كائن منه سبحانه وتعالى بخلقه وإرادته ما شاء كان وما لا فلا. والقضاء والقدر المراد بأحدهما الحكم الإجمالى وبالأخر التفصيلي.
And the summary of the matter is that al-qadr (destiny) is what occurs from the servant that was decreed in eternity from its good and evil, sweet and bitter, it is from Him, glorified and exalted, by His creation and will; what He wills is, and what He does not will is not. And al-qaḍā (decree) and al-qadr (destiny), one of them refers to the general judgment (al-ḥukm al-ijmālī), and the other to the detailed (al-tafṣīlī).
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a brief doctrinal definition. The author summarizes the concept of qadar: it is what happens to the servant that was determined in eternity, including all its aspects (good, evil, pleasant, bitter). This occurs by God's creation and will, in full accordance with His absolute sovereignty (what He wills is, what He does not will is not). Then the author distinguishes between qada and qadar: one is the general (ijmali) judgment, the other is the detailed (tafsili) judgment. This is a common distinction: qada is the overarching decree, while qadar is the specific determination of each thing's details.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think qada and qadar are synonymous with no distinction.
Correction: In this passage, the author explicitly distinguishes them: one is general judgment (qada), the other is detailed judgment (qadar).

Key Takeaway
Qadar is the eternal decree of all things; qada is the general judgment, while qadar is the detailed specification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The passage says both qada and qadar are from God. Have you ever attributed a good event to your own effort and a bad event to 'bad luck'? How does this lesson call you to repent from that?

Divine Writing: Descriptive, Not Prescriptive
The author distinguishes Allah's writing as descriptive, not prescriptive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah's writing in the Preserved Tablet is descriptive of what will be, not a command that creates instantly.

Core Idea
Allah's decree is a record of what will be, not a command that forces.

The lesson begins by clarifying a crucial distinction: when Allah wrote in the Preserved Tablet, He described future events rather than issuing a creative command. This understanding protects against misunderstandings about divine predestination and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the Pen
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the distinction between descriptive and prescriptive writing.
The passage uses a logical argument: if it were a command, all things would exist instantly.
2
Understand the distinction
Focus on the key contrast between 'bi al-wasf' (by description) and 'bi al-hukm' (by command). Reflect on why this distinction matters for understanding divine decree.
3
Connect to the hadith and Quran
Relate the passage to Imam Abu Hanifa's statement and the Quranic verse. Note how the hadith is derived from the Quran.
The hadith about the Pen supports the descriptive nature of writing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكن كتبه بالوصف لا بالحكم، أي كتب الله في حق كل شيء بأنه سيكون كذا وكذا لم يكتب بأنه ليكُن كذا وكذا، وتوضيحه أن وقت الكتابة لم تكن الأشياء موجودة، فكتب في اللوح المحفوظ على وجه الوصف أنه ستكون الأشياء على وفق القضاء لا على وجه الأمر بأنه ليكن؛ لأنه لو قال ليكن لكانت الأشياء كلها موجودةً حينئذ لعدم تصور تخلف المخلوق عن الأمر الإيجادي للخالق. وقال الإمام الأعظم في كتابه "الوصية": نقر بأن الله تعالى أمر القلم بأن يكتب، وفي نسخة بأن اكتب، فقال القلم : ماذا أكتب يارب؟ فقال الله تعالى : اكتب ما هو كائن إلى يوم القيمة. لقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَكُلُّ شَيْءٍ فَعَلُوهُ فِي الزُّبُرِ وَكُلُّ صَغِيرٍ وَ كَبِيرٍ مُسْتَطَرْ﴾ [القمر: ٥٢-٥٣] ، انتهى . الحديث مقتبس من القرآن؛ لأنه صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم كان في معرض التبيان. ومجمل الأمر أن القدر وهو ما يقع من العبد المقدر في الأزل من خيره وشره وحلوه ومره كائن منه سبحانه وتعالى بخلقه وإرادته ما شاء كان وما لا فلا. والقضاء والقدر المراد بأحدهما الحكم الإجمالى وبالأخر التفصيلي، وأما قول المعتزلة: «لو كان الكفر بقضاء الله تعالى لوجب الرضا به لأن الرضا بالقضاء واجب، واللازم باطل؛ لأن الرضا بالكفر كفر، فثبت أن الكفر ليس بقضاء الله، فلم تكن جميع أفعال العباد بقضاء الله تعالى على ما ذهب إليه أهل السنة والجماعة»، فمدفوع بأن الكفر مقضي لا قضاء، والرضى إنما يجب بالقضاء دون المقضى. وتوضيحه : أن الكفر له نسبة إليه سبحانه وهي كونه خلقه على مقتضى حكمته ولا اعتراض عليه فى مشيئته، فإنه مالك الملك يتصرف فيه كيف يشاء، لا يتضرر بشيء كما لا ينتفع به، وله نسبة أخرى إلى المكلف، وهي وقوعه صفة له بكسبه واختياره، والاعتراض واقع عليه في فعله؛ لأنه أسخط مولاه واستحق العقوبة الدائمة في عقباه، هذا ومن رضي بكفر نفسه فقد كفر اتفاقاً، ومن رضي بكفر غيره ففيه اختلاف المشايخ، والأصح أنه لا يكفر بالرضا بكفر الغير إن كان لا يحب الكفر، ولكن يتمنى أن يسلب الله عنه الإيمان حتى ينتقم منه على ظلمه وإيذائه، كذا في "التاتارخانيه" ويؤيده قوله تعالى حكاية عن موسى: ﴿ رَبَّنَا اطْمِسْ عَلَى أَمْوَالِهِمْ وَاشْدُدْ عَلَى قُلُوبِهِمْ فَلَا يُؤْمِنُوا حَتَّى يَرَوُا الْعَذَابَ الْأَلِيمَ ﴾ [يونس : ٨٨].
But He wrote it by description, not by command, meaning Allah wrote concerning everything that it will be such and such, not that it should be such and such. The clarification is that at the time of writing, things were not existent, so He wrote in the Preserved Tablet in the manner of description that things will occur according to decree, not in the manner of command that it should be, for if He said 'let it be', all things would have come into existence then, because it is inconceivable that the creature would lag behind the creative command of the Creator. And Imam al-A'zam said in his book 'al-Wasiyyah': We affirm that Allah Almighty commanded the Pen to write, and in another version 'to write', so the Pen said: 'What shall I write, O Lord?' Allah Almighty said: 'Write what will be until the Day of Resurrection.' For His saying: 'And everything they did is in the scriptures, and every small and great is inscribed.' [al-Qamar 52-53]. End. The hadith is derived from the Quran, for he (peace and blessings be upon him) was engaged in explanation. The summary is that al-qadr, which is what occurs from the servant predestined in eternity, of its good and evil, sweet and bitter, comes from Him, exalted and high, by His creation and will: whatever He wills is, and whatever He does not will is not. And al-qada' and al-qadar: one of them means the summary judgment and the other the detailed judgment. As for the saying of the Mu'tazila: 'If disbelief were by the decree of Allah, it would be obligatory to be pleased with it because being pleased with the decree is obligatory, and the necessary consequence is false because being pleased with disbelief is disbelief, so it is established that disbelief is not by Allah's decree, so not all actions of servants are by Allah's decree as the Ahl al-Sunnah hold' — it is refuted by [the fact] that disbelief is a decreed thing (maqdi) not the decree (qada'), and contentment is only required with the decree, not with the decreed thing. Its clarification: disbelief has a relation to Him, exalted, namely that He created it in accordance with His wisdom, and there is no objection to His will, for He is the Owner of Sovereignty, disposing as He wills, unaffected by anything as He does not benefit from it; and it has another relation to the morally responsible person, namely its occurrence as an attribute of him by his acquisition and choice, and objection falls upon him for his action, because he angered his Master and deserved eternal punishment in the Hereafter. And whoever is pleased with his own disbelief has disbelieved unanimously, and whoever is pleased with another's disbelief, there is disagreement among the shaykhs; the correct view is that he does not disbelieve by being pleased with another's disbelief if he does not love disbelief, but he wishes that Allah would remove faith from him so that he might take vengeance on him for his oppression and harm; thus in 'al-Tatarkhaniyyah', and it is supported by His saying, narrating from Moses: 'Our Lord, obliterate their wealth and harden their hearts so that they will not believe until they see the painful punishment' [Yunus: 88].
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and defense of the Sunni doctrine of qada' and qadar (divine decree and destiny). It begins by clarifying that Allah's writing in the Preserved Tablet is descriptive, not prescriptive: He wrote what will be, not a command that it should be. If it were a command, everything would have been created instantly. Then it quotes Imam Abu Hanifa's statement from al-Wasiyyah about the Pen and the hadith derived from the Quran. The author then defines al-qadar as what occurs from the servant predestined from eternity, and it is from Allah by creation and will. He distinguishes between al-qada' (the summary judgment) and al-qadar (the detailed judgment). Next, he presents the objection of the Mu'tazila: if disbelief were by Allah's decree, then one must be pleased with it, but being pleased with disbelief is disbelief, so disbelief cannot be by decree. The refutation is that disbelief is al-maqdi (the decreed thing) not al-qada' (the decree itself); contentment is only required with the decree, not with the decreed thing. The author further explains that disbelief has two relations: to Allah as Creator by wisdom, and to the servant as his acquisition and choice. Blame falls on the servant for his choice. Then he addresses the ruling on being pleased with disbelief: being pleased with one's own disbelief is disbelief; being pleased with another's disbelief does not necessarily constitute disbelief if one does not love disbelief but wishes for punishment of the wrongdoer. This is supported by a quote from the Quran where Moses prays against Pharaoh and his people.

Key Terms
اللوح المحفوظ
الوصف
الحكم
القضاء
القدر

Key Takeaway
Allah's writing in the Preserved Tablet describes what will happen, not a command that forces its occurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's writing is descriptive, not prescriptive, then your past sins were not forced upon you. Look at a specific sin you committed: can you truly own it as your choice while trusting that Allah recorded it in His knowledge without commanding it? What does that mean for your repentance today?
?
The Pen wrote 'what will be until the Day of Resurrection'—including every detail of your life. How does knowing that this record is a description of your choices, not a script that overrides your will, change the way you approach your next decision?
?
When you face a trial, do you ever feel that 'it was written' means you had no escape? Reflect: if the writing is descriptive, could it be that your trial is the exact test your soul needed? Will you embrace it as Allah's wise description of your journey, or resent it as an unfair command?

The Reality of Qadr and Refutation of the Mu'tazila
Definition of qadr, distinction of qada/qadar, and refutation of Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni definition of qadr, the distinction between qada and qadar, and how to refute the Mu'tazila objection that divine decree would require approval of disbelief.

Core Idea
Disbelief is decreed by Allah as a created thing, but the decree itself is not to be equated with the decreed act.

In this lesson, we delve into the core of the debate on predestination. The author defines qadr, distinguishes between qada (summary) and qadar (detailed), and then presents the Mu'tazila objection: if disbelief is by Allah's decree, then one must be pleased with it, but being pleased with disbelief is disbelief. The refutation hinges on a critical distinction: between the decree (qada) and the decreed thing (maqdi).

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the definition of qadr and the distinction between qada and qadar.
Qadr is what occurs from the servant predestined in eternity.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila objection
Identify the Mu'tazila argument: if disbelief is by decree, one must be pleased with it, but that is impossible.
3
Learn the refutation
Focus on the key distinction: disbelief is maqdi (decreed thing) not qada (decree). Contentment is required with the decree, not the decreed thing.
The refutation also explains the two relations of disbelief: to Allah as Creator and to the servant as acquirer.

ولكن كتبه بالوصف لا بالحكم، أي كتب الله في حق كل شيء بأنه سيكون كذا وكذا لم يكتب بأنه ليكُن كذا وكذا، وتوضيحه أن وقت الكتابة لم تكن الأشياء موجودة، فكتب في اللوح المحفوظ على وجه الوصف أنه ستكون الأشياء على وفق القضاء لا على وجه الأمر بأنه ليكن؛ لأنه لو قال ليكن لكانت الأشياء كلها موجودةً حينئذ لعدم تصور تخلف المخلوق عن الأمر الإيجادي للخالق. وقال الإمام الأعظم في كتابه "الوصية": نقر بأن الله تعالى أمر القلم بأن يكتب، وفي نسخة بأن اكتب، فقال القلم : ماذا أكتب يارب؟ فقال الله تعالى : اكتب ما هو كائن إلى يوم القيمة. لقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَكُلُّ شَيْءٍ فَعَلُوهُ فِي الزُّبُرِ وَكُلُّ صَغِيرٍ وَ كَبِيرٍ مُسْتَطَرْ﴾ [القمر: ٥٢-٥٣] ، انتهى . الحديث مقتبس من القرآن؛ لأنه صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم كان في معرض التبيان. ومجمل الأمر أن القدر وهو ما يقع من العبد المقدر في الأزل من خيره وشره وحلوه ومره كائن منه سبحانه وتعالى بخلقه وإرادته ما شاء كان وما لا فلا. والقضاء والقدر المراد بأحدهما الحكم الإجمالى وبالأخر التفصيلي، وأما قول المعتزلة: «لو كان الكفر بقضاء الله تعالى لوجب الرضا به لأن الرضا بالقضاء واجب، واللازم باطل؛ لأن الرضا بالكفر كفر، فثبت أن الكفر ليس بقضاء الله، فلم تكن جميع أفعال العباد بقضاء الله تعالى على ما ذهب إليه أهل السنة والجماعة»، فمدفوع بأن الكفر مقضي لا قضاء، والرضى إنما يجب بالقضاء دون المقضى. وتوضيحه : أن الكفر له نسبة إليه سبحانه وهي كونه خلقه على مقتضى حكمته ولا اعتراض عليه فى مشيئته، فإنه مالك الملك يتصرف فيه كيف يشاء، لا يتضرر بشيء كما لا ينتفع به، وله نسبة أخرى إلى المكلف، وهي وقوعه صفة له بكسبه واختياره، والاعتراض واقع عليه في فعله؛ لأنه أسخط مولاه واستحق العقوبة الدائمة في عقباه، هذا ومن رضي بكفر نفسه فقد كفر اتفاقاً، ومن رضي بكفر غيره ففيه اختلاف المشايخ، والأصح أنه لا يكفر بالرضا بكفر الغير إن كان لا يحب الكفر، ولكن يتمنى أن يسلب الله عنه الإيمان حتى ينتقم منه على ظلمه وإيذائه، كذا في "التاتارخانيه" ويؤيده قوله تعالى حكاية عن موسى: ﴿ رَبَّنَا اطْمِسْ عَلَى أَمْوَالِهِمْ وَاشْدُدْ عَلَى قُلُوبِهِمْ فَلَا يُؤْمِنُوا حَتَّى يَرَوُا الْعَذَابَ الْأَلِيمَ ﴾ [يونس : ٨٨].
But He wrote it by description, not by command, meaning Allah wrote concerning everything that it will be such and such, not that it should be such and such. The clarification is that at the time of writing, things were not existent, so He wrote in the Preserved Tablet in the manner of description that things will occur according to decree, not in the manner of command that it should be, for if He said 'let it be', all things would have come into existence then, because it is inconceivable that the creature would lag behind the creative command of the Creator. And Imam al-A'zam said in his book 'al-Wasiyyah': We affirm that Allah Almighty commanded the Pen to write, and in another version 'to write', so the Pen said: 'What shall I write, O Lord?' Allah Almighty said: 'Write what will be until the Day of Resurrection.' For His saying: 'And everything they did is in the scriptures, and every small and great is inscribed.' [al-Qamar 52-53]. End. The hadith is derived from the Quran, for he (peace and blessings be upon him) was engaged in explanation. The summary is that al-qadr, which is what occurs from the servant predestined in eternity, of its good and evil, sweet and bitter, comes from Him, exalted and high, by His creation and will: whatever He wills is, and whatever He does not will is not. And al-qada' and al-qadar: one of them means the summary judgment and the other the detailed judgment. As for the saying of the Mu'tazila: 'If disbelief were by the decree of Allah, it would be obligatory to be pleased with it because being pleased with the decree is obligatory, and the necessary consequence is false because being pleased with disbelief is disbelief, so it is established that disbelief is not by Allah's decree, so not all actions of servants are by Allah's decree as the Ahl al-Sunnah hold' — it is refuted by [the fact] that disbelief is a decreed thing (maqdi) not the decree (qada'), and contentment is only required with the decree, not with the decreed thing. Its clarification: disbelief has a relation to Him, exalted, namely that He created it in accordance with His wisdom, and there is no objection to His will, for He is the Owner of Sovereignty, disposing as He wills, unaffected by anything as He does not benefit from it; and it has another relation to the morally responsible person, namely its occurrence as an attribute of him by his acquisition and choice, and objection falls upon him for his action, because he angered his Master and deserved eternal punishment in the Hereafter. And whoever is pleased with his own disbelief has disbelieved unanimously, and whoever is pleased with another's disbelief, there is disagreement among the shaykhs; the correct view is that he does not disbelieve by being pleased with another's disbelief if he does not love disbelief, but he wishes that Allah would remove faith from him so that he might take vengeance on him for his oppression and harm; thus in 'al-Tatarkhaniyyah', and it is supported by His saying, narrating from Moses: 'Our Lord, obliterate their wealth and harden their hearts so that they will not believe until they see the painful punishment' [Yunus: 88].
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and defense of the Sunni doctrine of qada' and qadar (divine decree and destiny). It begins by clarifying that Allah's writing in the Preserved Tablet is descriptive, not prescriptive: He wrote what will be, not a command that it should be. If it were a command, everything would have been created instantly. Then it quotes Imam Abu Hanifa's statement from al-Wasiyyah about the Pen and the hadith derived from the Quran. The author then defines al-qadar as what occurs from the servant predestined from eternity, and it is from Allah by creation and will. He distinguishes between al-qada' (the summary judgment) and al-qadar (the detailed judgment). Next, he presents the objection of the Mu'tazila: if disbelief were by Allah's decree, then one must be pleased with it, but being pleased with disbelief is disbelief, so disbelief cannot be by decree. The refutation is that disbelief is al-maqdi (the decreed thing) not al-qada' (the decree itself); contentment is only required with the decree, not with the decreed thing. The author further explains that disbelief has two relations: to Allah as Creator by wisdom, and to the servant as his acquisition and choice. Blame falls on the servant for his choice. Then he addresses the ruling on being pleased with disbelief: being pleased with one's own disbelief is disbelief; being pleased with another's disbelief does not necessarily constitute disbelief if one does not love disbelief but wishes for punishment of the wrongdoer. This is supported by a quote from the Quran where Moses prays against Pharaoh and his people.

Key Terms
القضاء
القدر
المقضي
كسب
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila claim that if Allah decrees disbelief, one must be pleased with it, but they confuse the decree with the decreed thing.
Correction: Disbelief is maqdi (decreed thing), not qada (decree). Contentment is required with the decree, not the decreed act.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction used to refute the Mu'tazila objection?
A) Between qada (decree) and maqdi (decreed thing).
B) Between good and evil deeds.
C) Between Allah's will and His command.
D) Between predestination and free will.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila objection fails because disbelief is a decreed thing (maqdi), and contentment is required with the decree (qada), not with the decreed act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see evil in the world, do you ever feel a silent resentment toward Allah, questioning why He decreed it? How does the distinction between the decree (qada) and the decreed thing (maqdi) help you trust His wisdom without excusing the evil? Are you willing to submit your confusion to this truth today?
?
The Mu'tazila argument sounds logical: if Allah decrees disbelief, then you must be pleased with it—but that leads to kufr. Have you ever felt that logic made you hesitant to fully accept predestination? What would it cost you to surrender that doubt and embrace the two-relation explanation, even if you don't fully understand it? Can you pray for that surrender now?
?
If you truly believe that every act of sin, including your own, is created by Allah yet acquired by your own choice, does that make you more humble or more fearful? Do you still judge others for their sins as if they alone are responsible, forgeting that Allah's wisdom is at work? Will you commit today to withholding judgment and instead turning your gaze to your own soul's accountability?

Divine Decree

Ruling on Being Pleased with Disbelief
Ruling on being pleased with disbelief: own vs. another's.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling on being pleased with one's own disbelief versus being pleased with another's disbelief, according to Sunni scholars.

Core Idea
Being pleased with one's own disbelief is disbelief, but being pleased with another's disbelief does not necessarily make one a disbeliever.

The final part of the passage addresses a related question: what about being pleased with disbelief? If one is pleased with his own disbelief, he disbelieves by consensus. But if he is pleased with another's disbelief, there is disagreement. The correct view, as supported by a Quranic verse, is that it is not disbelief if one does not love disbelief but rather desires punishment for the wrongdoer.

Guided Path
1
Read the ruling on one's own disbelief
Note the unanimous ruling: being pleased with one's own disbelief is disbelief.
2
Understand the disagreement on another's disbelief
Read the part about being pleased with another's disbelief. Note the disagreement among early scholars.
3
Learn the correct view and its support
Focus on the correct view: it is not disbelief if one does not love disbelief but wishes for punishment. Connect to the Quranic verse from Moses.
The verse shows Moses praying for the disbelievers' hearts to be hardened and punishment to come.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

How can you align your supplications with the prophetic example of hating falsehood but still having a detached, sincere desire for truth to prevail?

Human Voluntary Actions and Refutation of Jabriyya
The author asserts humans have voluntary actions, contra the Jabriyya who deny any human act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that humans possess real voluntary actions for which they are rewarded or punished, and how this refutes the Jabriyya's denial of all human agency.

Core Idea
Human actions are real and carry moral responsibility; the Jabriyya's denial collapses reward and punishment.

After establishing divine predestination, the author now addresses a critical corollary: human moral responsibility. If humans have no real actions, as the Jabriyya claim, then reward and punishment become meaningless. This passage asserts the reality of voluntary human actions and explicitly rejects the Jabriyya position, grounding the theological need for kasb (acquisition).

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's straightforward claim.
Notice the structure: first an affirmation, then a negation of the Jabriyya view.
2
Identify the Two Key Claims
The passage makes a positive assertion: servants have voluntary actions leading to reward/punishment. Then it negates the Jabriyya claim that there is no action at all (neither kasb nor khalq).
The technical terms ikhtiyāriyyah, kasb, and khalq are central.
3
Reflect on the Implications
Why does the author link voluntary actions to reward and punishment? Consider that without real human agency, divine justice would be compromised. The Jabriyya position is rejected precisely because it undermines moral accountability.
This connects to the broader Ash'ari/Maturidi doctrine of kasb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت ذلك فللعباد أفعال اختيارية يثابون عليها إن كانت طاعة، ويعاقبون عليها إن كانت معصية، لا كما زعمت الجبرية أن لا فعل للعبد أصلا لا كسبا ولا خلقا، وأن حركاته
And when you know that, then for the servants there are voluntary actions for which they are rewarded if they are obedience, and punished if they are disobedience, not as the Jabriyya claimed: that there is no action for the servant at all, neither acquisition nor creation, and that his movements...
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a brief but clear affirmation of human free will within the Ash'ari/Maturidi framework. The author states that after understanding the previous discussion about divine will, one should know that servants have voluntary actions (أفعال اختيارية) for which they receive reward or punishment accordingly. This is a direct refutation of the Jabriyya (fatalists), who completely deny any human action, whether acquisition (kasb) or creation (khalq). The sentence is cut off but the meaning is clear: the Jabriyya reduce humans to passive instruments, which contradicts the reality of divine recompense.

Key Terms

الثواب (al-thawāb)
العقاب (al-'iqāb)

الكسب (al-kasb)
الخلق (al-khalq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God creates all actions, humans cannot truly act and therefore reward/punishment are unjust.
Correction: The passage affirms that humans have voluntary actions (by acquisition) and are justly rewarded or punished, rejecting the Jabriyya denial of any human role.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of the Jabriyya regarding human actions?
A) Humans have full free will
B) Humans have no action at all, neither acquisition nor creation
C) Humans create their own actions
D) Humans are rewarded for their actions

Key Takeaway
Humans truly perform voluntary actions that are subject to divine reward or punishment, contrary to the Jabriyya who deny any real human agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking 'it was written for me' or 'it was out of my hands'? How does this lesson's affirmation of voluntary actions confront that excuse, and what will you do the next time that thought arises?
?
Your belief about human agency affects how you live. Are there daily decisions—big or small—where you act as if you have no real choice, blaming fate or circumstances? What would change if you fully owned that your voluntary actions are real and matter to Allah?
?
The Jabriyya claimed no action for the servant at all, erasing moral responsibility. Is there any area of your faith where you've passively accepted a teaching without examining its implications for your accountability? Commit to studying one aspect of your 'aqeedah that directly impacts how you live.

Divine Decree

Human Actions Are Voluntary – Refuting the Jabriyya
The author affirms voluntary actions and refutes the Jabriyya through empirical distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the existence of voluntary human actions is proven by simple observation and that the Jabriyya denial of human action is false.

Core Idea
We know we have free will from the difference between a voluntary strike and an involuntary tremor.

The author begins by affirming that humans have voluntary actions that are the basis for reward and punishment. He then refutes the Jabriyya who deny any human action, offering a simple empirical proof: we can distinguish between a deliberate movement and a reflex. This lesson examines that proof and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the contrast between the author's affirmation and the Jabriyya position.
The author uses a very direct style: 'this is false, because...'
2
Identify the two types of movement
The author compares 'حركة البطش' (movement of a fist/strike) and 'حركة الرعش' (movement of a tremor). Why are they different?
One is voluntary, the other involuntary.
3
Understand why the Jabriyya view is false
The author says we know the first is by choice and the second is compelled. This empirical distinction disproves the Jabriyya claim that humans have no action at all.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت ذلك فللعباد أفعال اختيارية يثابون عليها إن كانت طاعة، ويعاقبون عليها إن كانت معصية، لا كما زعمت الجبرية أن لا فعل للعبد أصلا لا كسبا ولا خلقا، وأن حركاته بمنزلة حركات الجمادات لا قدرة له عليها لا مؤثرة ولا كاسبة في مقام الاعتبار ولا قصد ولا إرادة ولا اختيار، وهذا باطل، لأنا نفرق بين حركة البطش وحركة الرعش، ونعلم أنّ الأول باختیاره دون الثاني لاضطراره. فإن قيل : بعد تعلق علم الله وإرادته الجبرُ لازم قطعا؛ لأنهما إما أن يتعلقا بوجود الفعل فيجب، أو بعدمه فيمتنع لامتناع انقلاب علمه سبحانه جهلاً، وامتناع تخلف مراده عن إرادته أصلاً، وحينئذ لا اختيار مع الوجوب والامتناع قطعا ؟ . فالجواب: أنه سبحانه يعلم ويريد أن العبد يفعله أو يتركه باختياره فلا إشكال في هذا المقال، وتحقيقه أن صرف العبد قدرته أو إرادته إلى الفعل كسب، وإيجاد الله تعالى الفعل عقيب ذلك خلق، فالله تعالى خالق والعبد كاسب، ومن أضل ممن يزعم أن الله شاء الإيمان من الكافر والطاعة من الفاجر ، والكافر شاء الكفر، والفاجر شاء الفجور، فغلبت مشيئتهما مشيئة الله سبحانه.
And when you have understood that, then the servants have voluntary actions for which they are rewarded if they are acts of obedience and punished if they are acts of disobedience, not as the Jabriyya claimed that there is no action for the servant at all, neither acquisition nor creation, and that his movements are like the movements of inanimate objects; he has no power over them, neither effective nor acquisitive, in the realm of consideration, nor intention, nor will, nor choice. And this is false, because we distinguish between the movement of a fist (striking) and the movement of a tremor, and we know that the first is by his choice while the second is not due to its being compelled. Then if it is said: After the attachment of Allah's knowledge and will, compulsion is necessarily required, because they either attach to the existence of the act, so it must occur, or to its non-existence, so it cannot occur, due to the impossibility of His knowledge turning into ignorance and the impossibility of His will being contravened. Accordingly, there is no choice with necessity and impossibility absolutely. The answer is: He, exalted, knows and wills that the servant does it or leaves it by his own choice, so there is no problem in this statement. The verification of this is that the servant's directing his power or will to the act is acquisition (kasb), and Allah's bringing the act into existence subsequent to that is creation (khalq). Allah is the Creator and the servant is the acquirer. And who is more astray than one who claims that Allah willed faith from the disbeliever and obedience from the sinner, but the disbeliever willed disbelief and the sinner willed sin, so their will overcame the will of Allah, exalted?
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a combined affirmation of human free will and a refutation of the Jabriyya (determinists) who deny any human action. The author begins by stating that servants have voluntary actions (af‘āl ikhtiyāriyya) for which they are rewarded or punished. This is in contrast to the Jabriyya who claim that humans have no action at all — no acquisition (kasb) and no creation (khalq) — and are like inanimate objects. The author proves this false by the simple empirical distinction between a voluntary movement (like striking a fist) and an involuntary one (like trembling from a tremor); we know the first is by choice, the second compelled. Then an objection is raised: if Allah's knowledge and will are attached to an action, then either they attach to existence (so it must occur) or to non-existence (so it cannot), making compulsion necessary and leaving no room for choice. The answer is that Allah knows and wills that the servant does or leaves the act by his own choice (bi ikhtiyārihī). So there is no contradiction. The author then clarifies the doctrine of kasb: the servant's directing his power or will to the act is called kasb (acquisition), and Allah's creation of the act subsequent to that is called khalq (creation). Thus, Allah is the Creator and the servant is the acquirer. Finally, the author condemns those who claim that Allah willed faith for the disbeliever and the disbeliever willed disbelief, as if their will overcomes Allah's will — this is being astray.

Key Terms
أفعال اختيارية
الجبرية
كسب
خلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Jabriyya believe that humans have no free will and are like inanimate objects.
Correction: We can distinguish between voluntary and involuntary movements, proving that humans do have free will.

Checkpoint
How does the author prove that humans have free choice?
A) By quoting the Quran
B) By logical reasoning that movements differ
C) By consensus of scholars
D) By the doctrine of kasb

Key Takeaway
The ability to distinguish between voluntary and involuntary movements proves that humans have free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself excusing a sin by saying, 'It was beyond my control' or 'Allah wrote it that way'? In light of this lesson, can you honestly call that sin anything less than a voluntary choice? Will you today take full ownership of one specific sin and repent with that accountability?
?
Think of the most difficult act of obedience you performed recently. Did you really believe it was your own effort, or did you feel like a passive tool? The lesson says you can distinguish between strike and tremor. When you pray, fast, or give charity, is it a voluntary strike or a compelled tremor? What will you do to make your worship more intentional?
?
The Jabriyya claim removes moral responsibility entirely. But you know the difference. When you next feel tempted to blame fate for a failure, will you instead admit it was your own choice? How will that change the way you seek forgiveness and resolve to improve?

Divine Will and Human Choice: The Doctrine of Kasb
The author answers the objection of compulsion and explains the kasb doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the doctrine of kasb reconciles divine will and human free will, and why the objection that divine knowledge necessitates compulsion is answered.

Core Idea
Allah wills that the servant chooses, so human choice coexists with divine will.

The author now addresses a critical objection: if Allah's knowledge and will cover all events, doesn't that force the human to act? He answers by clarifying that Allah knows and wills that the human acts by his own choice. Then he explains the doctrine of kasb: the servant acquires the act by directing his power, and Allah creates the act. This distinction resolves the apparent tension.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
The objection says that if Allah's knowledge and will are attached, compulsion is necessary. The answer is that Allah knows and wills that the human chooses freely.
Notice how the author does not deny divine will but reframes it.
2
Understand the kasb-khalq distinction
The author defines kasb as the servant's directing of power/will to the act, and khalq as Allah's creation of the act. This shows that while Allah creates, the human has a real role.
Kasb is not creating; it is a genuine contribution.
3
Reflect on the warning
The author condemns those who think human will overcomes Allah's will. This reinforces that divine will is supreme but does not negate human choice.

Human Actions Are Not Like Inanimate Movements
Refutes the view that humans have no real choice or acquisition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the distinction between voluntary and involuntary actions proves that humans have real acquisition (kasb) and choice (ikhtiyar), contrary to the claim that actions are purely passive.

Core Idea
We know we choose some actions and not others — that is the proof.

This passage tackles a stark position: that human actions are no different from the movements of lifeless objects, with no power, intention, or choice on our part. The author declares this false and provides a simple, intuitive proof: our own experience distinguishes actions that we perform by choice from those that happen to us involuntarily. This distinction is the bedrock of the Ash'ari concept of acquisition (kasb).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the rhythm: the claim is stated, then declared false, then proven.
The proof is very short — just one contrast.
2
Identify the opposing view
The passage first states a view that is rejected: that human actions are like inanimate movements, with no power, kasb, intention, will, or choice. Write this position in your own words.
This is the view of the Jahmiyya or some extreme Qadariyya positions.
3
Understand the proof
The proof is the distinction between a voluntary act (striking) and an involuntary one (trembling). Ask yourself: do I experience this difference? That inner awareness is the evidence.
The proof does not rely on scripture but on immediate human experience.
4
Connect to the lesson's core idea
Reflect on how this simple distinction answers the objection that humans are entirely passive. If we can tell the difference, then we must have some form of power and choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بمنزلة حركات الجمادات لا قدرة له عليها لا مؤثرة ولا كاسبة في مقام الاعتبار ولا قصد ولا إرادة ولا اختيار، وهذا باطل، لأنا نفرق بين حركة البطش وحركة الرعش، ونعلم أنّ الأول باختیاره دون الثاني لاضطراره
like the movements of inanimate objects, having no power over them, neither effective nor acquiring in the realm of consideration, nor intention, nor will, nor choice. This is false, because we distinguish between the movement of striking and the movement of trembling, and we know that the first is by his choice unlike the second due to his being compelled.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that human actions are entirely passive, like the movements of inanimate objects, with no power (qudra), no acquisition (kasb), no intention (qasd), no will (irada), and no choice (ikhtiyar). The author declares this view false (bāṭil) and provides a simple proof: we intuitively distinguish between a voluntary act (e.g., striking) and an involuntary tremor (e.g., shaking from fear or illness). The first is done by choice, the second by compulsion. This distinction demonstrates that humans do possess a capacity that is effective and acquisitive, contrary to the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb means humans have no real role in their actions — actions are entirely created by Allah and humans are merely passive.
Correction: This passage shows that the Ash'ari position does affirm a real distinction between voluntary and involuntary actions, and that kasb is a genuine acquisition that humans experience consciously.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the proof that humans have choice and acquisition?
A) Quranic verses show it.
B) Hadith mentions it.
C) The distinction between voluntary and involuntary movement.
D) The consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
The intuitive distinction between voluntary and involuntary actions is the basic proof that humans possess real acquisition (kasb) and choice (ikhtiyar).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment when you acted (e.g., helped someone) and a moment when something happened to you involuntarily (e.g., shaking from fear). How did your inner experience differ? Does that difference prove you had a real choice in the first? If you disregard this distinction, what becomes of your responsibility before Allah?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'I couldn't help it,' as if it were trembling rather than striking? What would change in your repentance if you fully owned that your action was by your own choice (bi-ikhtiyārihī)? Take one specific habit and ask yourself: is this more like striking or trembling?

God's Knowledge, Will, and Human Choice: The Kasb Response
Objection and answer on divine knowledge/will and human freedom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari theory of kasb resolves the apparent conflict between God's eternal knowledge and will, and human free choice.

Core Idea
God wills the act as chosen by the servant, not as compelled.

A powerful objection arises: if God knows and wills everything, how can humans have any real choice? This passage presents that challenge and then refutes it using the key distinction between acquisition (kasb) and creation (khalq). The answer shows that God's knowledge and will encompass human choice, not override it. This lesson unpacks that argument and the critical concept of kasb.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objection: God's knowledge and will would seem to make human action either necessary or impossible, leaving no room for choice. Read the first part of the passage and notice the logical claim.
The objection uses the terms 'obligatory' (wājib) and 'impossible' (mumtani').
2
Identify the answer's core claim
The answer says God knows and wills that the servant acts with his own choice. This is the key: God's knowledge and will are not for the act in isolation, but for the act as freely chosen by the human.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.
3
Learn the kasb distinction
The passage then defines kasb (acquisition) as the servant directing his power/will to the act, and khalq (creation) as God bringing the act into existence. Grasp how this preserves both divine sovereignty and human responsibility.
God creates; the servant acquires the moral quality of the act.
4
Consider the warning against qadariyya-like errors
The passage ends by rebuking those who think human will can overcome God's will. Reflect on why this is considered misguided in Ash'ari theology.
The warning reinforces that God's will is supreme, but not in a way that nullifies human choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : بعد تعلق علم الله وإرادته الجبرُ لازم قطعا؛ لأنهما إما أن يتعلقا بوجود الفعل فيجب، أو بعدمه فيمتنع لامتناع انقلاب علمه سبحانه جهلاً، وامتناع تخلف مراده عن إرادته أصلاً، وحينئذ لا اختيار مع الوجوب والامتناع قطعا ؟ . فالجواب: أنه سبحانه يعلم ويريد أن العبد يفعله أو يتركه باختياره فلا إشكال في هذا المقال، وتحقيقه أن صرف العبد قدرته أو إرادته إلى الفعل كسب، وإيجاد الله تعالى الفعل عقيب ذلك خلق، فالله تعالى خالق والعبد كاسب، ومن أضل ممن يزعم أن الله شاء الإيمان من الكافر والطاعة من الفاجر ، والكافر شاء الكفر، والفاجر شاء الفجور، فغلبت مشيئتهما مشيئة الله سبحانه.
If it is said: After the attachment of God's knowledge and will, compulsion (al-jabr) is necessarily entailed, because they either attach to the existence of the act and then it is obligatory, or to its non-existence and then it is impossible, due to the impossibility of His knowledge turning into ignorance, and the impossibility of His intended object ever failing to follow His will. And then there is no choice with obligation and impossibility at all? The answer: He, exalted, knows and wills that the servant performs it or leaves it by his choice, so there is no problem in this statement. And its verification is that the servant's directing his power or will to the act is kasb (acquisition), and God's bringing the act into existence after that is khalq (creation). God is the creator (khalīq) and the servant is the acquirer (kāsib). And who is more astray than one who claims that God willed faith from the disbeliever and obedience from the sinner, while the disbeliever willed disbelief and the sinner willed sin, so their will overcame the will of God?
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection claims that God's eternal knowledge and will necessitate compulsion (jabr): if God knows and wills an act, it must occur; if He knows and wills its absence, it cannot occur. Thus, human choice is negated. The answer clarifies that God's knowledge and will are not for the act in isolation, but for the act as performed by the servant with his own choice. God knows and wills that the servant does or leaves it by his ikhtiyār (choice). So no contradiction arises. The author then explains the doctrine of kasb: the servant's directing his power or will towards the act is acquisition (kasb), and God's creating the act subsequently is creation (khalq). Thus God is the Creator (Khāliq) and the servant is the acquirer (Kāsib). The answer concludes by warning against the misguided claim that human will overcomes God's will, which is impossible.

Key Terms
جبر
اختيار
علم الله
إرادة الله
كسب
خلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's eternal knowledge and will force humans to act, leaving no room for free choice.
Correction: God knows and wills that the servant acts by his own choice; divine knowledge and will encompass human choice, not negate it.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is the role of the servant in the act?
A) The servant creates the act.
B) The servant acquires (yaksibu) the act by directing his power.
C) The servant has no role.
D) The servant forces God to create the act.

Key Takeaway
God's eternal knowledge and will do not compel human action; rather, He knows and wills that the servant acts by his own choice, and the servant's role is acquisition (kasb) while God's role is creation (khalq).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your past mistakes, do you truly believe that you chose them, or do you sometimes seek comfort in blaming God's decree? How does the kasb doctrine force you to take full ownership of your sins?
?
If God knows and wills every detail of your life, including your moments of weakness, does that make you feel trapped in a script, or does it liberate you to trust that your choices matter within His plan? Be honest: which feeling dominates, and why?
?
The passage condemns those who think their will can overcome God's will. Are there areas in your life where you are subtly trying to assert your will against what you know God has commanded? What would it take for you to align your will with His, not out of compulsion, but as an act of kasb?
