Ibn al-Humam's Objection and the Ash'ari Answer of Kasb
Ibn al-Humam's objection and the Ash'ari kasb response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn al-Humam's objection to the Ash'ari view of human action and the Ash'ari response through the concept of kasb (acquisition).

Core Idea
God creates the act, the servant acquires it — no compulsion, no independent creation.

This passage presents a powerful objection to the Ash'ari position on human action, attributed to the Hanafi scholar Ibn al-Humam. He argues that if God creates power in the servant, and power inherently produces its effect, then either the servant is an independent creator or God's action is contradictory. The Ash'ari answer introduces the key concept of kasb: the act is created by God but attributed to the servant through acquisition, avoiding both compulsion and independent agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the section starting with 'فإن قيل' to understand the logical argument against the Ash'ari position. Notice the premise that power is essentially efficacious.
2
Understand the Mu'tazili conclusion
The objection leads to the conclusion that servants must be independent creators of their acts, otherwise it's compulsion. See how this is the Mu'tazila position.
3
Examine the Ash'ari answer
Read the answer beginning with 'فالجواب'. Note the introduction of kasb: the act is created by God but related to the servant's power only by acquisition, not by creation.
4
Resolve the two-agents problem
The second part clarifies that two powers can coexist if one is of invention (ikhtira') and the other of acquisition (iktisab). Understand the distinction.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how kasb allows for moral responsibility without attributing independent creative power to humans, preserving God's sole creative agency.
This is a key doctrine in Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن تحقيق المرام ما ذكره ابن الهمام في هذا المقام حيث قال: فإن قيل: لا شك أنه تعالى خلق للعبد قدرة على الأفعال، ولذا ندرك تفرقة بين الحركة المقدورة وهي الاختيارية وبين الرعدة الضرورية، والقدرة ليست خاصيتها إلا التأثير، أي إيجاد المقدور، فإن القدرة صفة تؤثر على وفق الإرادة. ويستحيل اجتماع مؤثرين مستقلين على أثر واحد، فوجب تخصيص عمومات النصوص السابقة بما سوى أفعال العباد الاختيارية، فيكونون مستقلين بإيجاد أفعالهم الاختيارية الحادثة بخلق الله تعالى، كما هو رأي المعتزلة، وإلا كان جبرا محضا فيبطل الأمر والنهى بقدرتهم. فالجواب أن الحركة مثلا كما أنها وصف للعباد ومخلوق للرب لها نسبة إلى قدرة العبد فسُمِّيت تلك الحركة باعتبار تلك النسبة كسبا بمعنى أنها مكسوبة للعبد ولم يلزم الجبر المحض إذ كانت متعلق قدرة العبد داخلةً فى اختياره، وهذا التعلق هو المسمى عندنا بالكسب، انتهى. وأما ما سبق من استحالة اجتماع مؤثرين على أثر واحد، فالجواب عنه: أن دخول مقدور تحت قدرتين إحداهما قدرة الاختراع والأخرى قدرة الاكتساب جائز، وإنما المحال اجتماع مؤثرين مستقلين على أثر واحد.
Then know that the realization of the goal is what Ibn al-Humam mentioned in this place, where he said: If it is said: There is no doubt that He has created for the servant a power (qudra) over actions, and therefore we perceive a distinction between the voluntary movement (which is within power) and the involuntary tremor. And power is nothing but efficacy, i.e., bringing the effect into existence. For power is an attribute that produces effect in accordance with will. And it is impossible for two independent agents (mu'aththiran) to converge upon one effect. Therefore it is necessary to specify the general texts from before to exclude the voluntary actions of servants, so that they are independent in bringing about their voluntary actions that occur by the creation of God, as is the view of the Mu'tazila. Otherwise it would be pure compulsion (jabr), nullifying commands and prohibitions, by their power. The answer is that movement, for example, as it is an attribute of the servants and a creation of the Lord, has a relation to the power of the servant. So that movement, by virtue of that relation, is called 'acquisition' (kasb), meaning that it is acquired by the servant. And pure compulsion does not follow, for it is the object of the servant's power, entering into his choice. And this relation is what we call kasb. End. As for the previous statement about the impossibility of two agents on one effect, the answer is: that an effect coming under two powers — one being the power of invention (ikhtira') and the other the power of acquisition (iktisab) — is permissible. Rather, the impossible is the convergence of two independent agents on one effect.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents a key objection and answer in the Ash'ari theory of action. The objection, attributed to Ibn al-Humam, argues that since God creates power in the servant, and power is essentially efficacious, and two independent agents cannot produce one effect, it follows that the servant must be independent in creating his own actions, otherwise it would be pure compulsion. Mu'tazila take this as proof for their position. The Ash'ari answer introduces the concept of kasb: the action is created by God but is related to the servant's power, and this relation is called 'acquisition.' The servant's power does not produce the action, but the action is acquired by the servant through his intention. The second part clarifies that it is permissible for one effect to be under two powers if one is the power of invention (God's) and the other is the power of acquisition (the servant's). The impossibility only applies to two independent agents both originating the same effect.

الكسب
الاختراع
الاكتساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that kasb implies the servant independently creates the act.
Correction: In Ash'ari thought, kasb means the act is acquired by the servant while being created by God; the servant does not create independently.

Checkpoint

A) The servant independently creates his actions.
B) God creates the action and the servant acquires it.
C) The servant's power is the sole cause.
D) God and the servant are two independent agents.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari doctrine of kasb teaches that God creates all actions, but humans acquire them through their choice, reconciling divine omnipotence with human responsibility.

How must this change the way you repent and take accountability for your sins? Commit to one practical step today.

The objection in this passage claims that if God creates all actions, then commands and prohibitions become meaningless. Do you live as if your choices matter to God? Examine your heart: is there any area where you feel powerless against your own habits, forgetting that your acquisition makes you responsible? What would it look like to reclaim that area by affirming both God's creation and your acquisition?

Temporal Power and the Basis of Moral Obligation
Defines temporal power and explains the basis of moral responsibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of temporal power (qudra) as a created attribute and why firm intention (qasd muṣammam) is the basis of moral obligation (taklif).

Core Idea
Accountability rests on intention, not on producing the act.

This passage from Sharh al-'Aqa'id clarifies a key distinction in Sunni theology: the power to act is created by God at the moment of intention, but what makes us accountable is the firm intention itself, not whether the act actually occurs. This insight resolves the apparent tension between divine power and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the author's own words present the definition and basis of taklif.
2
Identify the definition of temporal power
Locate the precise definition: an attribute created by God in the servant when intending an act with sound causes and instruments.
Note that power is not self-caused but divinely given.
3
Understand the basis of taklif
The passage states that after choice is created, moral obligation hinges on firm intention, not on effective power. Find the sentence that expresses this.
This is the central point of the lesson.
4
Reflect on Ibn al-Humam's observation
Ibn al-Humam argues that specifying one heart action (firm determination) repels the charge of compulsion. However, the passage notes that even this determination falls under God's general creation. Consider the tension.
This is an advanced nuance that shows the depth of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي "شرح العقائد" تعريف القدرة الحادثة في العبد بأنها: صفة يخلقها الله تعالى في العبد عند قصده اكتساب الفعل مع سلامة الأسباب والآلات، وبهذا يظهر أن مناط التكليف بعد خلق الاختيار للعبد هو قصده الفعل قصداً مصمماً طاعة كان أو معصية. وإن لم تؤثر قدرته في وجود الفعل لمانع هو تعلق قدرة الله التي لا يقاومها شيء بإيجاد ذلك. ومن هنا قال ابن الهمام رحمه الله : إن لزوم الجبر يندفع بتخصيص النصوص بإخراج فعل واحد قلبي، وهو العزم المصمم ، لكن فيه أن ذلك العزم المصمم داخل تحت الحكم المعمم، والله سبحانه أعلم.
And in Sharh al-'Aqa'id, the definition of the temporal power (qudra) in the servant is that it is an attribute that God creates in the servant when he intends to acquire an act, with the soundness of causes and instruments. And by this it is apparent that the basis of moral obligation (taklif), after the creation of choice in the servant, is his intending the act with a firm intention (qasd muṣammam), whether it be obedience or disobedience. Even if his power has no effect in the existence of the act due to an obstacle, which is the attachment of God's power — which nothing resists — to bring it into existence. And from here Ibn al-Humam said: The necessity of compulsion is repelled by specifying the texts by excluding one heart action, which is the firm determination ('azm muṣammam), but there is that this firm determination falls under the general ruling. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage defines qudra (power) as a quality created by God in the servant at the moment of intending an action, provided the necessary means are sound. This shows that the basis of taklif (accountability) is not the power to effect the action but rather the firm intention (qasd muṣammam) to do it. Even if the power does not produce the act due to God's overriding will, the intention alone is sufficient for reward or punishment. Ibn al-Humam then notes that this solves the problem of compulsion by specifying that the only act that is truly from the servant is the inner firm determination, and that act is excluded from the general texts that attribute all acts to God. However, he also notes that this firm determination itself is subject to God's general creation, so the problem remains.

Key Terms
القدرة الحادثة
التكليف
العزم المصمم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that moral accountability requires the power to actually produce the act; without effective power, there is no responsibility.
Correction: The passage clarifies that accountability rests on firm intention, not on effective power. Even if the servant's power does not bring about the act due to divine will, the intention alone suffices for reward or punishment.

Checkpoint

A) The actual occurrence of the act.
B) The firm intention to perform the act.
C) The power to create the act.
D) The absence of any obstacle.

Key Takeaway
Temporal power is a created attribute, and moral accountability is based on firm intention, not on the effective power to produce the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Baqillani: Divine and Human Power
Presents al-Baqillani's distinction on power attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Baqillani's distinction between God's power attaching to the act's essence and human power attaching to its moral description, and how this resolves the problem of divine and human agency.

Core Idea
God creates the act; we choose its moral quality.

Al-Baqillani offers a nuanced solution to one of the most challenging problems in Islamic theology: how can human actions be both created by God and morally accountable? This passage presents his key distinction between the essence of an act and its moral description. Through the example of slapping an orphan, we see how divine and human power can coexist without conflict.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the key terms asl al-fi'l and wasf.
2
Understand the distinction
Identify that God's power is attached to the act's essence (its existence), while the servant's power is attached to its moral description (obedience or disobedience).
They do not compete for the same effect.
3
Analyze the example
Consider the example of slapping an orphan. The physical act is from God, but whether it is discipline or harm depends on the servant's intention.
This illustrates the distinction clearly.
4
Reflect on al-Razi's statement
Al-Razi summarizes that man is compelled in the form of a free agent. Think about how this relates to the broader theological debate on free will.
This is the ultimate limit of human understanding according to al-Razi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ما اختاره هو قول الباقلاني رحمه الله من أئمة أهل السنة، إن قدرة الله تعالى تتعلق بأصل الفعل، وقدرة العبد تتعلق بوصفه من كونه طاعة أو معصية، فمتعلق تأثير القدرتين مختلف، كما في لطم اليتيم تأديبا ،وإيذاءً، فإن ذات اللطم واقعة بقدرة الله تعالى وتأثيره، وكونه طاعة على الأول ومعصية على الثاني بقدرة العبد وتأثيره لتعلق ذلك بعزمه المصمم. ولقد أنصف الإمام الرازي في تفسيره الكبير حيث قال: الإنسان مجبور في صورة مختار، وهو أنهى ما يمكن أن ينتهي إليه فهم البشر.
Then what he chose is the saying of al-Baqillani, one of the imams of Ahl al-Sunna: that the power of God is attached to the essence of the act (asl al-fi'l), and the power of the servant is attached to its description (wasf) of being obedience or disobedience. So the object of influence of the two powers is different, as in slapping an orphan—if it is discipline it is obedience, if it is harm it is disobedience. The essence of the slapping occurs by the power and influence of God, and its being obedience in the first case and disobedience in the second case is by the power and influence of the servant, due to its attachment to his firm determination. And indeed Imam al-Razi spoke fairly in his great Tafsir where he said: Man is compelled (majbur) in the form of a free agent (mukhtar), and this is the ultimate limit of human understanding.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents al-Baqillani's solution to the problem of divine and human agency. God's power affects the act itself (its existence), while the servant's power affects its moral description (whether it is obedience or disobedience). They are not competing for the same effect; therefore, they can coexist. The example: slapping an orphan can be either discipline (obedience) or harm (disobedience); the physical slap is from God, but its moral quality is determined by the servant's intention. Al-Razi then summarizes the human predicament: man is compelled in the form of a free agent, acknowledging that ultimately human freedom is limited by divine decree.

Key Terms
أصل الفعل
وصف الفعل
الطاعة
المعصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Baqillani denies any role for human power, making God the sole agent of all acts including their moral quality.
Correction: Al-Baqillani gives the servant power over the moral description of the act, not its existence. Humans determine whether an act is obedience or disobedience through their intention.

Checkpoint
According to al-Baqillani, what does the servant's power affect?
A) The existence of the act.
B) The act's moral description (obedience or disobedience).
C) Both the act and its description.
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
al-Baqillani resolves the problem of divine and human agency by distinguishing the act's essence (from God) from its moral quality (from human intention).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you truly believe that the act's existence came from God but its disobedience came from your own choice? How does this belief shape your repentance and your view of God's justice?
?
Think of an action you performed today that could have been either obedience or disobedience depending on your intention. What does that reveal about your personal responsibility before God?
?
Imam al-Razi says you are 'compelled in the form of a free agent'. Does your daily experience feel like compulsion or freedom? How can you live with the tension and still hold yourself accountable to God?

Ibn al-Humam's Attempt to Avoid Compulsion
Ibn al-Humam's proposal to avoid compulsion and its rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn al-Humam's proposal to resolve the problem of compulsion by specifying a single heart-action and the critique that undermines it.

Core Idea
One free heart-action cannot escape the general divine rule.

Ibn al-Humam offers a clever way to avoid compulsion: single out one heart-action as free. But does it work? The passage presents his view and an objection showing the difficulty of escaping divine determinism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms: جبر, عزم مصمم, تخصيص النصوص, حكم معمم.
The passage is short but dense; each word carries theological weight.
2
Identify Ibn al-Humam's proposal
Ibn al-Humam says the necessity of compulsion is repelled by specifying the general texts, excluding one heart-action: firm resolve (العزم المصمم). Understand that he tries to carve out an exception for human free will.
This is an attempt to reconcile divine control and human responsibility.
3
Understand the critique
The author objects that this firm resolve itself is included under the general ruling (الحكم المعمم). That means it too is subject to God's will, so the problem of compulsion returns.
The critique shows the difficulty: if God's ruling covers everything, no exception is possible.
4
Reflect on the unresolved issue
The author ends with 'والله سبحانه أعلم', acknowledging the debate remains open. Reflect on why this is a key issue in the qadar discussion.
This passage illustrates the fine-tuning needed in arguments about predestination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هنا قال ابن الهمام رحمه الله : إن لزوم الجبر يندفع بتخصيص النصوص بإخراج فعل واحد قلبي، وهو العزم المصمم ، لكن فيه أن ذلك العزم المصمم داخل تحت الحكم المعمم، والله سبحانه أعلم.
And from here Ibn al-Humam, may God have mercy on him, said: The necessity of compulsion is repelled by specifying the texts by excluding one heart-action, which is firm resolve. But there is in it (the objection) that this firm resolve is included under the general ruling. And God Almighty knows best.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage reports an opinion of Ibn al-Humam. He attempts to avoid the problem of compulsion (al-jabr) by arguing that the general texts about divine control can be specified by excluding one internal action of the heart: firm resolve (al-azm al-musammam). By making this one act free, compulsion is avoided. However, the author notes a critique: that firm resolve itself is still included under the general divine ruling (al-hukm al-mu'ammin), meaning it too is subject to God's will and power, so the problem of compulsion is not truly solved. The author concludes with 'God knows best', indicating the issue remains debatable.

Key Terms
جبر
عزم مصمم
تخصيص النصوص
حكم معمم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ibn al-Humam's solution is accepted uncritically by the author.
Correction: The author explicitly raises a counter-argument, showing that the proposed exception (firm resolve) itself falls under the general divine rule, so the solution is not successful.

Checkpoint
What is the critique of Ibn al-Humam's suggestion?
A) The heart-action is not real.
B) The firm resolve itself falls under the general divine rule.
C) The texts cannot be specified.
D) It leads to pure compulsion.

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Humam's attempt to avoid compulsion by exempting firm resolve fails because firm resolve itself is under divine ruling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a firm resolve to do something good, do you sincerely acknowledge that even that resolve is entirely under God's control? Or do you secretly feel that this one decision is fully your own?
?
Ibn al-Humam tried to carve out a space for absolute human freedom, but the passage shows it collapses. Are there areas in your life where you still cling to a sense of autonomy, refusing to see God's hand over even your most private intentions?
?
If every act, including your deepest heart's resolve, is under God's general ruling, how does that change the way you pray for steadfastness? Do you truly depend on Him for the very will to obey?

Al-Baqillani's Distinction of Divine and Human Power
Al-Baqillani's distinction between divine power over the essence of the act and human power over its moral description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Baqillani's solution to the problem of divine and human agency, distinguishing the essence of an act from its moral description.

Core Idea
God creates the act; humans create its moral value.

Al-Baqillani, a leading Ash'ari theologian, offers a subtle resolution to the apparent conflict between divine omnipotence and human responsibility. Rather than dividing the act itself, he distinguishes between the act's essence (which comes from God) and its moral quality (which arises from human intention). This lesson unpacks his argument using the example of slapping an orphan.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's own wording settle before interpreting.
2
Identify the two distinct objects of power
Notice that God's power is linked to 'asl al-fi'l' (the essence of the act), while human power is linked to 'wasf' (the description of obedience or disobedience).
These are two different aspects, not two different acts.
3
Analyze the example of slapping an orphan
The same physical slap can be either discipline (obedience) or harm (disobedience). The slap itself is from God, but its moral quality is from the human's intention.
4
Reflect on the role of 'firm resolve' (ʿazm muṣammam)
The passage links the moral description to the servant's firm resolve. This shows that human responsibility is tied to intention, not just the physical action.
This preserves moral accountability without making humans creators.
5
Consider how this resolves the problem of human responsibility
Al-Baqillani's distinction allows both divine omnipotence (God creates all acts) and human responsibility (humans choose the intention). Think about why this is a clever theological solution.
This is a key Ash'ari position on qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ما اختاره هو قول الباقلاني رحمه الله من أئمة أهل السنة، إن قدرة الله تعالى تتعلق بأصل الفعل، وقدرة العبد تتعلق بوصفه من كونه طاعة أو معصية، فمتعلق تأثير القدرتين مختلف، كما في لطم اليتيم تأديبا ،وإيذاءً، فإن ذات اللطم واقعة بقدرة الله تعالى وتأثيره، وكونه طاعة على الأول ومعصية على الثاني بقدرة العبد وتأثيره لتعلق ذلك بعزمه المصمم.
Then what he chose is the saying of al-Baqillani, may God have mercy on him, one of the imams of Ahl al-Sunna: that the power of God Almighty relates to the essence of the act, and the power of the servant relates to its description of being obedience or disobedience. Thus the object of influence of the two powers is different, as in the example of slapping an orphan disciplinarily and harmfully: the essence of the slap occurs by the power of God Almighty and His influence, and its being obedience in the first case and disobedience in the second is by the power of the servant and his influence, because that is attached to his firm resolve.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents the view of al-Baqillani, a leading Ash'ari theologian. He solves the conflict by assigning different aspects of the action to divine and human power. God's power creates the essence of the action (asl al-fi'l), while the human's power determines its moral description (wasf) as obedience or disobedience. The example given is slapping an orphan: if done for discipline, it is obedience; if done to harm, it is disobedience. The slap itself is from God, but its moral quality arises from the servant's firm intention (azm musammam). This preserves human responsibility without making the human a true creator.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God creates both the act and its moral quality, so humans are not responsible for sin.
Correction: Al-Baqillani limits human power to the moral description only; the act's essence is from God, but humans choose the intention, making them responsible for the moral quality.

Checkpoint
According to al-Baqillani, what does the servant's power affect?
A) The essence of the action.
B) The description of the action as obedience or disobedience.
C) Both the essence and the description.
D) Neither; all is from God.

Key Takeaway
Al-Baqillani's distinction allows humans to be truly responsible for the moral quality of their actions while affirming that God alone creates the act itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God creates the essence of every action, including your sins, does that make you less accountable? Al-Baqillani insists you are fully responsible for the moral description. How does this teaching change the way you approach repentance—do you blame God for the act or take ownership of your intention?
?
Consider a time you blamed circumstances or your nature for a wrong action. According to this lesson, the sinfulness arises from your firm resolve. Will you now examine your intentions before you act and take full ownership of the moral quality of your deeds, knowing that God has given you the power to choose obedience or disobedience?

Imam al-Razi's Paradox: Compelled in the Form of a Free Agent
Al-Razi's summary: man is compelled in the form of a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on human action as captured by Imam al-Razi's paradoxical formulation.

Core Idea
Human action is divinely compelled yet experienced as free.

Imam al-Razi, in his monumental Tafsir al-Kabir, encapsulates the Ash'ari doctrine of human action in a single memorable sentence. This lesson unpacks his formulation and its significance for understanding divine predestination and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Take note of the two key terms: مجبور (compelled) and مختار (free agent).
2
Understand the paradox
Consider how al-Razi combines these opposites. 'Compelled in the form of a free agent' means that in reality God determines all actions, but humans experience themselves as free choosers.
This is the Ash'ari kasb (acquisition) theory summarized.
3
Reflect on the limit of human understanding
Al-Razi says this is the ultimate limit of human understanding. Why might he say that? Reflect on the mystery of divine decree.
4
Avoid common misunderstanding
Some think al-Razi is saying humans are purely compelled with no choice. But the phrase 'in the form of a free agent' preserves human responsibility.
The commentary clarifies this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقد أنصف الإمام الرازي في تفسيره الكبير حيث قال: الإنسان مجبور في صورة مختار، وهو أنهى ما يمكن أن ينتهي إليه فهم البشر.
And indeed Imam al-Razi was fair in his Great Exegesis, where he said: Man is compelled in the form of a free agent, and it is the ultimate limit that human understanding can reach.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage quotes Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi from his Tafsir al-Kabir. He summarizes the Ash'ari position on free will and predestination with a paradox: 'Man is compelled (majbur) in the form of a free agent (mukhtar).' In other words, from a divine perspective, all actions are determined by God, but from a human perspective, we experience choice and are accountable. Al-Razi states that this is the furthest that human understanding can reach on this issue. The author of the commentary endorses this as a fair assessment.

Key Terms
مجبور
مختار
فهم البشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi is admitting that humans have no free will at all.
Correction: Al-Razi's phrase 'in the form of a free agent' indicates that humans do experience choice and are responsible, even though ultimately compelled by divine decree.

Checkpoint
What does Imam al-Razi mean by 'man is compelled in the form of a free agent'?
A) Man has no choice at all.
B) Man is truly free.
C) The divine decree determines all, but man experiences choice.
D) It is an unsolvable mystery.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi's statement teaches that the Ash'ari position on human action is a mystery: humans are divinely compelled yet perceive themselves as free agents, and this is the limit of human understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in a good deed, do you truly attribute its creation to Allah, or do you secretly credit your own will? How will you remind yourself tomorrow that even your 'free choice' is a form of divine compulsion?
?
If man is 'compelled in the form of a free agent,' then your sins are also created by Allah. Does this make you more humble about your faults, or more desperate for His mercy? What will you change in your repentance tonight?
?
Al-Razi says this paradox is the limit of human understanding. When you face a trial you cannot reconcile with divine justice, do you submit as a 'free agent' trusting Allah, or do you rebel as if you truly had independent power? Which āyah or hadith will you cling to in that moment?

All Acts Are by God's Will: Obedience and Disobedience
Affirms that all human acts are by God's will, and explains the status of obedience and disobedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all human acts, whether obedience or disobedience, occur by God's will and decree, but that God loves and is pleased only with obedience.

Core Idea
God wills all acts, but loves only obedience.

This passage from the commentary on Fiqh al-Akbar affirms a core Ash'ari principle: everything that happens, including human actions, is under God's will and decree. But it carefully distinguishes between what God wills and what He loves. Obedience is both willed and beloved; disobedience is willed but not beloved. Let's examine the text to see how this distinction is made.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its full translation. Notice how the commentator expands the core statement about actions being by God's will, knowledge, decree, and predestination.
The passage is dense, but the structure is clear: a general statement, then specifications for obedience and disobedience.
2
Distinguish obedience from disobedience
Identify the sections on obedience and disobedience. For obedience, note the multiple attributes: commanded by God, loved by Him, pleasing to Him, and also by His knowledge, will, decree, and predestination. For disobedience, note that it is only by His knowledge, decree, predestination, and will.
The text lists Quranic verses to support each attribute for obedience.
3
Reflect on the theological distinction
Ask yourself: Why does God will disobedience if He does not love it? The passage implies that God's will encompasses all events, but His love and pleasure are reserved for what aligns with His command.
This distinction is crucial in Ash'ari theology to avoid attributing imperfection to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي) أي أفعال العباد (كلها ) أي جميعها من خيرها وشرها، وإن كانت مكاسبهم (بمشيئته) أي بإرادته وعلمه) أي بتعلق علمه وقضائه وقدره أي على وفق حكمه وطبق قدر تقديره، فهو مريد لما يسميه شراً من كفر ومعصية، كما هو مريد للخير من إيمان وطاعة. والطاعات (كلها أي جنسها بجميع أفرادها الشامل لواجبها وندبها (ما كانت) أي قليلة أو كثيرة (واجبةً) أي ثابتة بأمر الله تعالى) أي بإقامتها في الجملة حيث قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَأَطِيعُوا اللهَ وَأَطِيعُوا الرَّسُولَ﴾ [المائدة: ٩٢] . وبمحبته) أي لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ يُحِبُّ الْمُتَّقِينَ﴾ [آل عمران: ٧٦]، ﴿وَاللهُ يُحِبُّ الْمُحْسِنِينَ﴾ [آل عمران: ١٣٤]، ﴿وَيُحِبُّ المتطهرين﴾ [البقرة: ٢٢٢] (وبرضائه أي لقوله تعالى في حق المؤمنين : ﴿رَضِيَ اللهُ عَنْهُمْ وَرَضُوا عَنْهُ﴾ [البينة: ٨]، (وعلمه) أي لتعلق علمه سابقا في عالم الشهود، وتحققه لاحقاً في عالم الوجود (ومشيئته) أي بإرادته (وقضائه أي حكمه وتقديره) أي بمقدار قدّره أولاً، وكتبه في اللوح المحفوظ وحرّره ثانياً، وأظهره في عالم الكون وقرّره ثالثاً، ثم يجزيه جزاء وافيا في عالم العقبى رابعا. والمعاصى كلها أي صغيرها وكبيرها (بعلمه) وقضائه وتقديره ومشيئته إذ لو لم يردها
And 'they' meaning the actions of the servants, 'all of them' meaning all of them, their good and evil, even if they are their acquisitions, are 'by His will' meaning by His intention, 'and His knowledge' meaning by attachment of His knowledge, 'and His decree and His predestination' meaning in accordance with His judgment and the measure of His determination. So He wills what is called evil, like disbelief and disobedience, just as He wills good, like faith and obedience. And the acts of obedience, all of them meaning their kind including all individuals encompassing obligatory and recommended, 'were' meaning few or many, 'obligatory' meaning established by the command of God Almighty, meaning by establishing them in general where God Almighty said 'And obey God and obey the Messenger' [Quran 5:92]. 'And by His love' meaning by His saying 'Indeed God loves the righteous' [Quran 3:76], 'and God loves the doers of good' [Quran 3:134], 'and He loves those who purify themselves' [Quran 2:222]. 'And by His pleasure' meaning by His saying concerning the believers 'God is pleased with them and they are pleased with Him' [Quran 98:8]. 'And His knowledge' meaning by attachment of His knowledge earlier in the world of witnessing and its realization later in the world of existence. 'And His will' meaning by His intention, 'and His decree' meaning His judgment, 'and His predestination' meaning by the measure He determined first, wrote in the Preserved Tablet and recorded second, manifested in the world of existence and decided third, then will reward it with full recompense in the world of the Hereafter fourth. And the acts of disobedience, all of them, meaning their small and great, are 'by His knowledge, and His decree, and His predestination, and His will, for if He did not will them...'
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the phrase 'They (actions of servants) are all by His will, knowledge, decree, and predestination.' The commentator explains that 'they' refers to all actions, good and evil, and they are all under God's will and knowledge. He then tackles the specific status of obedience and disobedience. For acts of obedience, they are obligatory by God's command, beloved by Him, and He is pleased with them, citing Quranic verses. For acts of disobedience, they are also by His knowledge, decree, predestination, and will. The commentator emphasizes that God wills both good and evil, but He loves and is pleased only with obedience. The passage also breaks down the process of divine decree: God determines, writes in the Preserved Tablet, manifests in existence, and rewards in the hereafter. The final incomplete sentence begins to argue that if God did not will disobedience, it would not occur.

Key Terms
مشيئة
علم
قضاء
قدر
طاعة
معصية
محبة
رضا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because God wills disobedience, He must also love it or approve of it.
Correction: The passage clarifies that while God wills both obedience and disobedience, His love and pleasure are only for obedience, not for disobedience.

Checkpoint
What is the status of acts of obedience according to this passage?
A) They are willed by God but not commanded.
B) They are commanded, beloved, and pleasing to God.
C) They are only known by God.
D) They are not willed by God.

Key Takeaway
All human actions occur by God's will and decree, but only obedience is beloved and pleasing to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever dismiss it by thinking, 'It was written, so it's not my fault'? But this lesson shows that although Allah wills both obedience and disobedience, He only loves obedience. Does knowing that your sin is willed but not loved by Allah make you more determined to repent and seek His love through obedience? What specific sin will you abandon today for His sake?
?
Think of an act of obedience you performed recently. Did you recognize that it was not only willed by Allah but also loved and pleased by Him? How does knowing that Allah loves your obedience motivate you to increase your good deeds, even in difficulty? Write down one small act of worship you will do daily this week, and commit to it as a response to His love.
?
The lesson states that Allah wills disbelief and disobedience just as He wills faith and obedience. Reflect on a time you struggled with a trial or a temptation. Did you realize that your action was under Allah's decree, but He only loves the choice of obedience? Are you content to let His will be done, or do you actively strive to make your actions accord with His love? Pray now for the strength to choose what He loves.

Human Capacity and the Basis of Obligation
Author defines human capacity and refutes predestination as an excuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of human capacity (qudra) that makes one eligible for religious obligation, and why predestination is not a valid excuse for failing to obey.

Core Idea
Ability is the soundness of means, not a claim against divine decree.

The author begins by grounding religious obligation in human capacity, citing the Quranic principle that Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity. He then defines the specific ability required for accountability and gives examples of exemptions. This lesson clarifies that genuine incapacity is physical or mental, not a plea of predestination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the Quranic citation and the definition.
2
Identify the definition of capacity
Find where the author defines 'ability of the servant' (qudrat al-‘abd). Notice that it is defined as 'soundness of the instrument'.
This is the key concept.
3
Examine the examples and conclusion
Look at the examples: children, insane, mute, sick. Then see the application to Abu Jahl and the negligent believer. Understand why the author says they cannot claim inability.
The conclusion is that predestination is not an excuse.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن الطاعة بحسب الطاقة كما قال الله تعالى: ﴿لَا يُكَلِّفُ اللَّهُ نَفْسًا إِلَّا وُسْعَهَا﴾ [البقرة: ٢٨٦]، أي قدرتها. وقدرة العبد التي يصير بها أهلاً لتكليف الطاعة هي: سلامة الآلة التي بها يؤدي ما يجب عليه من المعرفة والعبادة فلذا لا يكلف الصبي والمجنون بالإيمان، ولا الأخرس بالإقرار باللسان، ولا المريض العاجز عن القيام بالقيام في مقام الإحسان؛ فكان أبو جهل غير مسلوب العقل، ولم يكن له أن يقول: لا أقدر على أن أصدق وأعترف وكذا المؤمن الصحيح التارك للصلاة ليس له أن يقول: لا أقدر أن أصلي.

والحاصل أن العبد ليس له أن يعتذر ويتعلق بالقضاء والقدر وفيه إشكال مشهور ذكرناه في تفسير قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا سَوَاءٌ عَلَيْهِمْ أَأَنْذَرْتَهُمْ أَمْ لَمْ تُنْذِرْهُمْ لَا يُؤْمِنُونَ﴾ [البقرة: ٦] حيث نزلت هذه الآية في قوم بأعيانهم علم الله منهم أنهم لا يؤمنون كأبي جهل وأبي لهب وغيرهما، ووجه الإشكال ظاهر، حيث أمرهم بالإيمان مع تقرّر علمه بأنهم يموتون على الكفر. والجواب: أن إيمانهم ليس محالاً لذاته بل لغيره، حيث تعلق علم الله بعدمه، فهم في عدم إيمانهم عاصون من وجه وطائعون من وجه ولعل هذا المعنى يستفاد من قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَهُ أَسْلَمَ مَنْ فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ طَوْعًا وَكَرْهًا﴾ [آل عمران: ۸۳]، أي انقاد فيما أراد رب العباد، وسر القدر مخفي على البشر في الدنيا، بل في العقبى فتدبر. قال الله تعالى: ﴿قُلْ فَلِلَّهِ الْحُجَّةُ الْبَالِغَةُ فَلَوْ شَاءَ لَهَدَىكُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ﴾ [الأنعام: ١٤٩].
Then know that obedience is according to capacity, as Allah, the Exalted, said: {Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity} [Al-Baqarah 2:286], meaning its ability. And the ability of the servant by which he becomes qualified for the obligation of obedience is: the soundness of the instrument by which he performs what is obligatory upon him of knowledge and worship. For this reason, the child and the insane are not obliged to belief, nor the mute to verbal confession, nor the patient unable to stand to stand in the station of excellence. Thus Abu Jahl was not deprived of reason, and it was not for him to say: 'I am not able to be truthful and confess.' Similarly, the healthy believer who abandons prayer is not entitled to say: 'I am not able to pray.' And the conclusion is that the servant is not to make excuses and cling to decree and predestination. And in this is a well-known problem which we mentioned in the interpretation of His saying, the Exalted: {Indeed, those who disbelieve – it is the same to them whether you warn them or do not warn them; they will not believe} [Al-Baqarah 2:6], for this verse was revealed about specific people whom Allah knew would not believe, like Abu Jahl, Abu Lahab, and others. And the aspect of the objection is apparent: He commanded them to believe while His knowledge was established that they would die upon disbelief. And the answer: Their disbelief is not impossible in itself, but due to something else, because Allah's knowledge attached to its non-occurrence. Thus, in their non-belief they are disobedient from one aspect and obedient from another aspect. Perhaps this meaning is derived from His saying, the Exalted: {And to Him submits whoever is in the heavens and the earth, willingly or unwillingly} [Al 'Imran 3:83], meaning they submitted in what the Lord of servants willed. And the secret of predestination is hidden from humans in this world, indeed in the afterlife, so reflect. Allah, the Exalted, said: {Say, 'To Allah belongs the conclusive argument. If He had willed, He would have guided you all'} [Al-An'am 6:149].
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage contains two main parts: a discussion of human capacity for obligation, and a resolution of a famous objection regarding predestination. The author first establishes that obligation (taklif) is according to capacity (tawqa), citing the Qur'anic verse that Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity. The capacity (qudra) that makes a servant eligible for obligation is defined as the soundness of the means (salamat al-ala) by which one performs obligatory acts of knowledge and worship. Hence, certain individuals are exempt: children, the insane, the mute, and the sick. Applying this, the author argues that Abu Jahl was not mentally incapacitated, so he could not claim inability to believe. Similarly, a healthy believer who abandons prayer cannot claim inability to pray. The author then states that no one can use predestination as an excuse, but acknowledges a well-known problem: the verse about the disbelievers (2:6) was revealed for specific people whom Allah knew would never believe, yet He still commanded them to believe. The objection is that commanding them seems futile. The author's answer is that their disbelief is not impossible in itself (muhal li-dhatihi) but only impossible due to God's knowledge that it would not occur. Thus, in their disbelief, they are disobedient in one respect (because they go against the command) and obedient in another (because they conform to God's will). The author then references the verse that all creatures submit to God's will, willingly or unwillingly, and concludes that the secret of divine decree is hidden from humans, urging reflection.

Key Terms
طاقة
قدرة
تكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God decrees one's actions, one can claim inability to obey.
Correction: The author clarifies that ability is not about decree but about having the physical and mental means. One cannot use predestination as an excuse.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the definition of the ability that qualifies a person for religious obligation?
A) The ability to do whatever one wants
B) The soundness of the means by which one performs obligatory knowledge and worship
C) The absence of any divine decree preventing action
D) The possession of wealth and health

Key Takeaway
True ability for obligation is the soundness of one's faculties, not freedom from divine decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you miss a prayer or neglect an act of worship, what excuse do you tell yourself? Based on this lesson, is that excuse valid, or are you like the 'healthy believer' who cannot claim inability?
?
Abu Jahl had sound faculties but chose disbelief. You have sound faculties and know the truth – where is there still disobedience in your life that you blame on 'I just can't'? What will you do about it starting today?
?
If true inability is only the lack of sound instrument, then every time you have the ability and still disobey, you are responsible. Can you honestly say you have no willful sins left unrepented? How does this accountability sit with your heart?

The Qadar Objection Resolved: God's Command and Foreknowledge
Author raises and resolves the objection about commanding disbelievers known to persist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the famous objection about God commanding disbelievers He knows will never believe, and the author's answer that their disbelief is impossible due to God's knowledge, not in itself.

Core Idea
Disbelief is not impossible in itself, but only by God's knowledge.

The author anticipates a classic theological objection: If God knows that certain people will never believe, why does He command them to believe? This lesson presents the objection, rooted in the verse about the disbelievers, and the author's nuanced answer that distinguishes between impossibility in essence and impossibility due to divine foreknowledge. The discussion concludes with the hidden nature of divine decree and a reminder of God's conclusive argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage describing the objection
Read the Arabic passage from 'والحاصل' to the end. Notice how the author introduces the well-known problem and the verse it is based on.
2
Understand the author's answer
Focus on the phrase 'ليس محالاً لذاته بل لغيره'. This is the key distinction. The author says their disbelief is not impossible in itself but due to God's knowledge.
This resolves the objection.
3
Reflect on the concluding verses
Read the final verses about submission and the secret of predestination. Think about why the author ends with 'فتدبر' (reflect).
The secret of divine decree is hidden.

Obedience According to Capacity
Defines capacity for moral obligation and applies it to examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that moral obligation is based on the capacity defined as soundness of the instrumental means, not on actual performance.

Core Idea
Capacity for obligation is about soundness of means, not ability to perform.

In this passage, the author clarifies a key principle in Islamic theology: Allah does not oblige anyone beyond their genuine capacity. But what does 'capacity' mean? The passage distinguishes between having the necessary faculties to be held accountable and actually performing the act. This distinction resolves common misunderstandings about responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation, to grasp the overall argument and examples.
2
Identify the Quranic verse and definition
Notice the verse from Surah al-Baqarah and how the author defines 'capacity' for obligation as soundness of the instrument.
The instrument refers to the faculties needed for knowledge and worship.
3
Note the exempted categories
List the groups not obligated: child, insane, mute, sick. Why is each exempted?
Each lacks a specific faculty: intellect, speech, physical ability.
4
Understand the inexcusable cases
Consider Abu Jahl and the negligent believer. Why can't they claim incapacity?
Abu Jahl had intellect, the believer had sound faculties. Having the means makes one accountable.
5
Reflect on the principle
The key takeaway: capacity for obligation is about having the necessary faculties, not about actual performance. This prevents excuses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن الطاعة بحسب الطاقة كما قال الله تعالى: ﴿لَا يُكَلِّفُ اللَّهُ نَفْسًا إِلَّا وُسْعَهَا﴾ [البقرة: 286]، أي قدرتها. وقدرة العبد التي يصير بها أهلاً لتكليف الطاعة هي: سلامة الآلة التي بها يؤدي ما يجب عليه من المعرفة والعبادة فلذا لا يكلف الصبي والمجنون بالإيمان، ولا الأخرس بالإقرار باللسان، ولا المريض العاجز عن القيام بالقيام في مقام الإحسان؛ فكان أبو جهل غير مسلوب العقل، ولم يكن له أن يقول: لا أقدر على أن أصدق وأعترف وكذا المؤمن الصحيح التارك للصلاة ليس له أن يقول: لا أقدر أن أصلي.
Then know that obedience is according to capacity, as Allah Most High said: 'Allah does not charge a soul except [with that within] its capacity' (2:286), i.e., its ability. And the ability of the servant by which he becomes worthy of the obligation of obedience is: the soundness of the instrument by which he performs what is incumbent upon him of knowledge and worship. Therefore, the child and the insane person are not charged with faith, nor the mute with affirmation by the tongue, nor the sick person unable to stand with standing in the station of excellence. So Abu Jahl was not deprived of intellect, and it was not for him to say: 'I am not able to believe and acknowledge.' Likewise, the sound believer who abandons prayer does not have the right to say: 'I am not able to pray.'
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage defines the principle of capacity in moral obligation. It distinguishes between the capacity that makes one eligible for obligation (soundness of means) and the capacity to actually perform the action. It gives examples of those who are not obligated due to lack of capacity (child, insane, mute, sick) and then applies it to Abu Jahl and a negligent believer to show that they cannot claim incapacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Capacity for moral obligation is measured by one's actual ability to perform the act.
Correction: Capacity for obligation is defined by the soundness of the instrumental faculties (mind, speech, physical ability), not by the actual performance.

A) Abu Jahl
B) A sound believer
C) A child or insane person
D) A mute person speaking on his own?

Key Takeaway
Moral obligation depends on having the necessary faculties; lacking them excuses, but having them makes one accountable for failure to perform.

The Problem of Commanding Faith with Foreknowledge
Presents and answers the objection about commanding faith with foreknowledge of disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the famous theological objection regarding God's command to believe in those He knows will not believe, and the answer that distinguishes between intrinsic and extrinsic impossibility.

Core Idea
God's foreknowledge does not make the command absurd; it only makes the act impossible extrinsically.

This lesson tackles a classic objection in Islamic theology: How can God command belief in people He knows will never believe? The author presents the objection using the example of Abu Jahl and Abu Lahab, and offers a nuanced answer that preserves both divine knowledge and human accountability. The key distinction is between something being impossible in itself versus impossible due to an external factor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing too much. Then read the English translation. Notice how the author sets up the problem and then answers it.
Pay attention to the Quranic verses cited.
2
Identify the objection and the answer
Locate where the objection is stated (beginning with 'وجه الإشكال ظاهر') and where the answer begins ('والجواب:'). Understand the reasoning.
The answer uses the phrase 'محال لذاته' vs 'محال لغيره'.
3
Reflect on the distinction and its implications
Think about the difference between something being 'impossible for itself' and 'impossible due to another'. Why does this preserve human responsibility?
The author ends with 'سر القدر مخفي', reminding us of the limits of human understanding.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن العبد ليس له أن يعتذر ويتعلق بالقضاء والقدر وفيه إشكال مشهور ذكرناه في تفسير قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا سَوَاءٌ عَلَيْهِمْ أَأَنْذَرْتَهُمْ أَمْ لَمْ تُنْذِرْهُمْ لَا يُؤْمِنُونَ﴾ [البقرة: 6] حيث نزلت هذه الآية في قوم بأعيانهم علم الله منهم أنهم لا يؤمنون كأبي جهل وأبي لهب وغيرهما، ووجه الإشكال ظاهر، حيث أمرهم بالإيمان مع تقرّر علمه بأنهم يموتون على الكفر. والجواب: أن إيمانهم ليس محالاً لذاته بل لغيره، حيث تعلق علم الله بعدمه، فهم في عدم إيمانهم عاصون من وجه وطائعون من وجه ولعل هذا المعنى يستفاد من قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَهُ أَسْلَمَ مَنْ فِي السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ طَوْعًا وَكَرْهًا﴾ [آل عمران: 83]، أي انقادوا فيما أراد رب العباد، وسر القدر مخفي على البشر في الدنيا، بل في العقبى فتدبر. قال الله تعالى: ﴿قُلْ فَلِلَّهِ الْحُجَّةُ الْبَالِغَةُ فَلَوْ شَاءَ لَهَدَاكُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ﴾ [الأنعام: 149].
The conclusion is that the servant has no right to excuse himself and cling to decree and destiny. And in it there is a famous problem which we mentioned in the interpretation of His saying, Exalted is He: 'Indeed, those who disbelieve - it is all the same to them whether you warn them or do not warn them; they will not believe' (2:6), when this verse was revealed concerning specific people whom Allah knew would not believe, like Abu Jahl and Abu Lahab and others. The aspect of the problem is apparent: He commanded them to believe while His knowledge was determined that they would die in disbelief. The answer: Their faith is not impossible in itself but due to something else, because Allah's knowledge of its non-existence attached to it. So in their non-belief they are disobedient in one aspect and obedient in another. Perhaps this meaning is derived from His saying: 'And to Him submits whoever is in the heavens and the earth, willingly or unwillingly' (3:83), i.e., they submitted in what the Lord of servants willed. And the secret of destiny is hidden from humans in this world, rather in the afterlife, so reflect. Allah said: 'Say, "To Allah belongs the conclusive argument. If He had willed, He would have guided you all."' (6:149)
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents a theological difficulty: How can God command people to believe while knowing they will not believe? The answer is that disbelief of these individuals is not intrinsically impossible but becomes impossible due to God's knowledge that it will not occur. Therefore, they are blameworthy because they are not forced, and the command is valid. The author also stresses that the secret of predestination is hidden.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's foreknowledge of disbelief makes His command to believe unjust or absurd.
Correction: The passage corrects this by explaining that the impossibility is not intrinsic but arises from God's knowledge; the command remains valid and humans are responsible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the faith of Abu Jahl not impossible in itself?
A) Because he had free will
B) Because it is impossible only due to God's knowledge of its non-occurrence
C) Because God forced him
D) Because the command was abrogated

Key Takeaway
God's command remains valid even when He foreknows disbelief because the impossibility is extrinsic, not intrinsic, preserving human accountability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used the excuse 'it was my fate' to avoid taking responsibility for a wrong choice? How does this lesson's distinction between intrinsic and extrinsic impossibility challenge that excuse? What step will you take today to own a decision you have been blaming on destiny?
?
If you truly believe that God's command remains valid even when He knows you will disobey, how should that change the way you respond to His commands? What is one command of Allah that you have been treating lightly, and what will you do to obey it today?
?
The secret of destiny is hidden from us. Are you willing to trust God's justice and wisdom in matters you do not understand, such as why some people are guided and others are not? What specific area of your life—a relationship, a trial, or a disappointment—do you need to surrender to His plan right now?
